Sins Are Repaid Like for Like
Commentator explains recompense of sins and transfer scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sins are recompensed proportionally, except for unbelief which leads to eternal punishment, and that God may pardon sins or transfer them in cases of oppression.

Core Idea
God repays sin with its like, but His mercy can pardon—except for unbelief.

This lesson unpacks the first half of a poetic line from the Jawharat al-Tawhid. The commentator defines what a sin is, explains its recompense, and discusses a unique case where sins are transferred due to oppression. Understanding this helps clarify divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage on sins once, then its translation. Notice the poetic line and the commentator's breakdown.
2
Identify the definition of sin
The commentator defines sayyi'a as anything blameworthy in the Law, whether minor or major. Note the etymology: the doer will be distressed at resurrection.
This definition is key to understanding recompense.
3
Explore recompense and pardon
God may punish sin by its like or pardon it. The exception is unbelief (kufr), which leads to eternal fire. Ask: why is unbelief excluded?
Pardon is a mercy; justice would require punishment.
4
Understand the transfer of sins
If an oppressor's good deeds are exhausted, the victim's sins are transferred to the oppressor. This shows the complexity of divine recompense.
This is based on hadith about settling accounts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي تحفة المريد على جوهرة التوحيد الحسناتُ مُضاعفة والسيئاتُ بمثلها] ١٠١ - فالسَّيِّئاتُ عِنْدَهُ بِالمِثْلِ والحَسَناتُ ضُوْعِفَتْ بِالفَضْلِ قوله : فالسَّيِّنَاتُ عِنْدَهُ بالمِثْل ) أي : جزاؤها عنده تعالى مُقدَّر بمثلها ) ، إن جازاه عليها، وله أن يعفو عنها إن لم تكن كُفراً، وإلا خُلد في النار، والسيئات جمع سيئة؛ ما يُذمُّ فاعله شرعاً ، صغيرة كانت أو كبيرة، وسُميت سيئة؛ لأن فاعلها يساء عند المقابلة عليها يوم القيامة، والمراد التي عملها العبد حقيقة أو حكماً بأن طرحت عليه لظلامة الغير بعد نفاد حسناته، فإنه يُؤخذ من حسنات الظالم ويُعطى للمظلوم، فإذا نفدت حسنات الظالم طرح عليه من سيئات المظلوم، ثم قُذف بالظالم في النار . وقوله: (والحَسَناتُ ضُوْعِفَتْ بالفَضْلِ) أي : ضاعفها الله تعالى بفضله لا وجوباً عليه، والحسنات جمع حسنة؛ وهي ما يمدح فاعله شرعاً، وسميت حسنةٌ لحُسن وجه صاحبها عند رؤيتها يوم القيامة . والمراد الحسنات المقبولة الأصلية المعمولة للعبد أو ما في حكمها بأن عَمِلَها عنه غيره، كما إذا تصدَّق غيرك عنك بصدقةٍ لا المأخوذة في نظير ظلامةٍ، فخرج بــ ( المقبولة) المردودة بنحو رياء فلا ثواب فيها أصلاً، وبـ (الأصلية) الحاصلة بالتضعيف (۲) فلا تضاعف ثانياً، وبـ (المعمولة أو ما في حكمها ) (۳) الحسنةُ التي هَمَّ
It is Tuhfat al-Mureed 'ala Jawharat al-Tawhid. 'Good deeds are multiplied and evil deeds are like for like.' 101. 'So evil deeds with Him are like for like, and good deeds are multiplied by grace.' His saying: 'So evil deeds with Him are like for like' means their recompense with the Exalted is measured by their like, if He punishes for them, and He may pardon them if they are not unbelief, otherwise he remains eternally in the Fire. 'Evil deeds' (sayyi'at) is plural of sayyi'a: that for which the doer is blameworthy according to the Law, whether minor or major. They are called sayyi'a because the doer is distressed when confronted with them on the Day of Resurrection. What is meant are those which the servant actually performed or effectively (by being placed upon him due to another's oppression after his good deeds are exhausted). For from the oppressor's good deeds are taken and given to the oppressed, and when the oppressor's good deeds are exhausted, some of the oppressed's evil deeds are placed upon him, then the oppressor is thrown into the Fire. His saying: 'And good deeds are multiplied by grace' means God multiplies them by His grace, not out of obligation upon Him. 'Good deeds' (hasanat) is plural of hasana: that for which the doer is praised according to the Law. They are called hasana because of the beauty of the doer's face when seeing it on the Day of Resurrection. What is meant are the accepted, original good deeds performed by the servant or those in their stead (done by another on his behalf, like someone giving charity on your behalf), not those taken in exchange for oppression. Thus excluded by 'accepted' are those rejected due to hypocrisy, etc., which have no reward at all; by 'original' are those obtained by multiplication, so they are not multiplied a second time; by 'performed or in their stead' is the good deed that one intends...
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a poetic line from the Jawharat al-Tawhid about the recompense of sins and good deeds. The author first explains 'evil deeds are like for like': God may punish for a sin with a proportionate punishment unless He pardons, except for unbelief which leads to eternal punishment. He defines 'evil deed' as anything blameworthy in the Law, small or large, and notes the term's etymology (the doer is distressed at confrontation). He also includes effective evil deeds—those transferred from the oppressed to the oppressor when the oppressor's good deeds run out, leading to the oppressor being cast into Hell. Then he explains that good deeds are multiplied 'by grace,' meaning God does this voluntarily, not out of obligation. He defines 'good deed' as something praiseworthy in the Law, with the etymology of beauty at resurrection. He further specifies that the multiplied good deeds must be 'accepted, original, and performed by the servant or in his stead'—excluding rejected deeds (e.g., from hypocrisy), already multiplied deeds, and those done by another without authorization. The passage ends mid-sentence with the good deed one intends, likely to be continued in the next lesson.

جزاء
مثل
عفو
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must punish every sin without exception.
Correction: The passage states God may pardon sins (except unbelief), showing His mercy alongside justice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if an oppressor's good deeds are exhausted?
A) The oppressor is forgiven.
B) The victim's sins are transferred to the oppressor.
C) The oppressor's punishment is doubled.
D) The victim's sins are cancelled.

Key Takeaway
Sins are repaid by their like, but God may pardon except for unbelief, and sins can transfer from victims to oppressors when good deeds run out.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have wronged someone and your good deeds are few. What will you do now to ensure you do not end up carrying their sins? Will you seek their forgiveness before the Day when no good deeds remain?
?
Are there sins you assume are 'too small' to matter? The passage says both minor and major are sin. Which 'small' sin are you currently excusing, and how will you repent from it today?
?
If you knew that every sin you committed would confront you with distress on the Day of Resurrection, what would you change about your actions right now? Write down one sin you will abandon starting today.

Good Deeds Are Multiplied by Grace
Commentator explains multiplication of good deeds by grace and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that good deeds are multiplied by God's grace, not obligation, and that only certain types of good deeds are subject to multiplication: accepted, original, and performed or authorized.

Core Idea
God multiplies good deeds freely by grace, not out of necessity.

The second half of the poetic line addresses good deeds. The commentator explains that their multiplication is an act of divine grace, not a requirement. He then specifies which good deeds qualify for multiplication, excluding those that are rejected, already multiplied, or obtained through oppression. This lesson clarifies the nature of God's generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage on good deeds and its translation. Note the phrase 'by grace' and the conditions that follow.
2
Understand multiplication by grace
The commentator emphasizes that God multiplies good deeds by His grace, not because He is obligated. This highlights His generosity.
This counters the idea that God must reward.
3
Identify the definition of a good deed
A hasana is an act for which the doer is praised in the Law, named for the beauty of the doer's face at resurrection.
4
Analyze the three conditions for multiplication
The commentator lists three qualifiers: the good deed must be accepted (not rejected like from hypocrisy), original (not already multiplied), and performed by the servant or done on his behalf (like charity by another).
Note what each condition excludes.

Avoiding Major Sins and the Forgiveness of Minor Sins
The author explains that avoiding major sins leads to forgiveness of minor sins and lists the seven destructive sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that avoiding major sins with repentance leads to the forgiveness of minor sins, and you will know the seven destructive sins that are considered major.

Core Idea
Repentance from major sins opens the door for minor sins to be forgiven.

This lesson begins the section on 'Audible Matters' (al-samiyyat), which are theological truths known from revelation. The author explains a fundamental principle: avoiding major sins—which includes repenting from them—results in the expiation of minor sins. He also lists the seven most destructive sins that believers must be especially cautious of.

Guided Path
1
Read the primary verse and hadith
Read the source passage focusing on the Quranic verse (4:31) and the hadith about the seven major sins.
The verse is the main proof for the principle.
2
Understand the definition of major sins
The author defines major sins as great sins in terms of punishment, and clarifies that 'avoiding' includes repentance after committing.
This avoids the misconception that one must never commit any major sin.
3
Identify the seven destructive sins
List the seven sins mentioned: shirk, sorcery, murder, consuming orphan's wealth, usury, fleeing battle, and slandering chaste women.
The author says the seven are not a limit; other major sins are similar.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this teaching encourages repentance and highlights God's mercy.
The hadith describes the reward of eight gates of Paradise.

السَّمْعِيَّاتُ
١٠٢ - وَبِاجْتِنَابِ لِلكَبَائِرُ تُغْفَرُ صَغَائِرٌ وَجَا الْوُضُوْ يُكَفِّرُ
قوله : (وَبِاجْتِنَابِ للكَبَائِرُ) بِسُكُونِ الرَّاءِ لِأَنَّهُ رَجَزُ، وَالْمُرَادُ بِاجْتِنَابِ الْكَبَائِرِ مَا يَعُمُّ التَّوْبَةَ مِنْهَا بَعْدَ فِعْلِهَا، لَا مَا يَخُصُّ عَدَمَ ارْتِكَابِهَا بِالْمَرَّةِ بِخِلَافِ التَّلَبُّسِ بِهَا مِنْ غَيْرِ تَوْبَةٍ، وَالْكَبَائِرُ هِيَ الذُّنُوبُ الْعَظِيمَةُ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْمُؤَاخَذَةُ بِهَا . وَقوله : (تُغْفَرُ صَغَائِرُ) أَيْ : تُكَفَّرُ الذُّنُوبُ الصَّغَائِرُ، قَالَ تَعَالَى : ﴿إِنْ تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنْكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ﴾ [النِّسَاء: (۳۱) ] أَيِ الصَّغَائِرَ، وقَالَ : مَا مِنْ عَبْدٍ يُؤَدِّي الصَّلَوَاتِ الْخَمْسِ وَيَصُومُ رَمَضَانَ وَيَجْتَنِبُ الْكَبَائِرَ السَّبْعَ إِلَّا فُتِحَتْ لَهُ ثَمَانِيَةُ أَبْوَابِ الْجَنَّةِ يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ، حَتَّى إِنَّهَا لَتَصْفُقُ - أَيْ: يَضْرِبُ بَعْضُهَا بَعْضًا مِنْ خُلُوِّهَا - فَلَا يَدْخُلُهَا أَحَدٌ حَتَّى يَدْخُلَهَا (٥) . وَالسَّبْعُ لَيْسَتْ بِقَيْدٍ بَلْ غَيْرُهَا كَذَلِكَ وَالْمُرَادُ بِهَا الْمُوبِقَاتُ السَّبْعُ؛ وَهِيَ : الشِّرْكُ بِاللَّهِ، وَالسِّحْرُ، وَقَتْلُ النَّفْسِ بِغَيْرِ حَقٍّ، وَأَكْلُ مَالِ الْيَتِيمِ، وَأَكْلُ الرِّبَا، وَالتَّوَلِّي يَوْمَ الزَّحْفِ، وَقَذْفُ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ الْغَافِلَاتِ.
The Audible Matters. 102 - And by avoiding the major sins, minor sins are forgiven, and wudu' came to expiate. He said: 'And by avoiding the major sins' with sukun on the ra because it is rajaz. What is meant by avoiding the major sins includes repentance from them after committing them, not specifically not committing them at all, in contrast to persisting in them without repentance. The major sins are the great sins in terms of the punishment for them. And His saying: 'minor sins are forgiven' meaning: the minor sins are expiated. God said: 'If you avoid the major sins from what you are forbidden, We will expiate for you your misdeeds' [Quran 4:31] i.e., the minor sins. And he said: 'There is no servant who performs the five prayers, fasts Ramadan, and avoids the seven major sins except that eight gates of Paradise will be opened for him on the Day of Resurrection, so much so that they will flap—meaning: some of them strike others because of their emptiness—and no one will enter them until he enters them' (5). The seven are not a restriction, rather others are likewise. The intended are the seven destructive sins: polytheism with God, sorcery, killing a soul without right, consuming the wealth of an orphan, consuming usury, turning back on the day of battle, and slandering chaste, oblivious women.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a section on the 'Audible Matters' (al-samiyyat), i.e., matters known from revelation. The author presents a line of poetry: 'And by avoiding the major sins, minor sins are forgiven, and wudu' came to expiate.' He then comments on this line. First, he notes the grammatical point: 'by avoiding the major sins' has a sukun on the ra because it is in rajaz meter. The meaning of 'avoiding major sins' includes repenting from them after having committed them; it does not mean never committing them at all. Persisting in major sins without repentance is not what is intended. Major sins (kaba'ir) are defined as great sins in terms of the punishment for them. Next, he explains that 'minor sins are forgiven' means they are expiated. He cites the verse from Surah al-Nisa' (4:31) where avoiding major sins leads to expiation of misdeeds (the minor sins). He then mentions a hadith: any servant who performs the five prayers, fasts Ramadan, and avoids the seven major sins will have eight gates of Paradise opened for him, so that the gates flap due to emptiness until he enters. The author clarifies that the mention of seven is not a restriction; other major sins are also included. He lists the seven destructive sins (al-mubiqat al-sab'): associating partners with God (shirk), sorcery (sihr), killing a person unjustly, consuming the wealth of orphans, consuming usury (riba), turning back on the day of battle, and slandering chaste, unsuspecting women.

Key Terms
كبائر
صغائر
تكفير
السبع الموبقات
توبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Avoiding major sins means never committing them at all.
Correction: The author clarifies that it includes repentance after committing, so even if one has committed a major sin, repentance allows one to be among those who 'avoid' major sins.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the definition of major sins (kaba'ir)?
A) Sins that are greater than minor sins in number.
B) Sins that are great in terms of punishment.
C) Only the seven specific sins listed.
D) Sins that cannot be forgiven.

Key Takeaway
Avoiding major sins—with repentance—causes minor sins to be forgiven, and the seven most destructive sins must be especially avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the seven destructive sins are you most vulnerable to, and what concrete steps will you take this week to avoid it with sincere repentance?
?
Have you been neglecting repentance from major sins, assuming minor sins are automatically forgiven? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you change today?
?
If the gates of Paradise are opened for those who avoid the seven major sins, what is holding you back from making a firm commitment to avoid them, knowing that repentance is always available?

Multiplication of Good Deeds (Tadh'if)
Explains the rule of multiplication of good deeds and its specifics for this ummah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rule of multiplication of good deeds, including that bad deeds are not multiplied, the exception for compensation, and that multiplication is a special characteristic of this ummah with varying degrees based on sincerity.

Core Idea
Good deeds are multiplied, bad deeds are not — a special mercy for this ummah.

This passage from the matn explains a key distinction in Islamic eschatology: good deeds are multiplied, while bad deeds are recorded as single. It also clarifies an exception and emphasizes that this multiplication is a unique blessing for the Muslim ummah, with degrees varying according to sincerity. Let's read the original text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation to get a general understanding.
Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the three key rules
The passage presents three main points: (1) bad deeds are not multiplied, (2) an exception for the compensation good deed, (3) multiplication is specific to this ummah with degrees.
Notice that the exception is introduced by 'wa biqawliha la'.
3
Reflect on the degrees of multiplication
The least is ten, but it can go up to seven hundred or more, depending on sincerity and intention. Ask yourself: How does this encourage righteous deeds?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات بها، فتكتب واحدةً من غير تضعيف، وكذلك إذا صَمَّم على المعصية ثم تركها، فله حسنةٌ من غير مضاعفة. وبقولنا : (لا) المأخوذة في نظير ظلامة الحسنة التي يأخذها المظلوم من ظالمه فلا تضاعف. والتضعيف من خصائص هذه الأمة، وأما غيرها من الأمم فكانت حسنتهم بحسنةٍ واحدةٍ وأقل مراتب التضعيف عشرة، وقد تضاعف إلى سبعين إلى سبعمئة أو أكثر من غير انتهاء إلى حَدٌ تقف عنده، وتفاوت مراتب التضعيف بحسب ما يقترن بالحسنة من الإخلاص وحسن النية.
The sam'iyyat (matters known from revelation): As for it, it is written as one without multiplication, and likewise if one resolves upon a sin then abandons it, he has a good deed without multiplication. And by our saying 'no' taken in the analogy of the compensation of the good deed which the wronged person takes from his oppressor, it is not multiplied. And multiplication is a characteristic of this ummah; as for other nations, their good deed was one good deed, and the least degree of multiplication is ten, and it may be multiplied to seventy, to seven hundred, or more without ending at a limit at which it stops. And the levels of multiplication vary according to what accompanies the good deed of sincerity and good intention.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage explains the rule of multiplication (tadh'if) of good deeds. It begins by stating that a bad deed is recorded as one without multiplication; even the intention to sin that is abandoned counts as a single good deed without multiplication. Then it clarifies an exception: the good deed taken as compensation by the oppressed from his oppressor is not multiplied. Multiplication is a special characteristic of the Muslim ummah; previous nations received only one reward per good deed. The minimum multiplication for this ummah is ten, and it can increase to seventy, seven hundred, or more without an upper limit, varying according to the sincerity and good intention accompanying the deed.

Key Terms
تضعيف
مضاعفة
حسنة
سيئة
إخلاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bad deeds are multiplied as well.
Correction: The text explicitly states that bad deeds are recorded as one without multiplication.

Checkpoint
What is the minimum multiplication of a good deed for this ummah?
A) One
B) Ten
C) Seventy
D) Seven hundred

Key Takeaway
The multiplication of good deeds is a special mercy for this ummah, and its degree depends on sincerity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that even a small good deed can be multiplied up to seven hundred times or more depending on your sincerity. Think about the last good deed you did—was it done with full sincerity for Allah alone? What specific change can you make today to ensure your next deed is purely for Him, so that its reward multiplies greatly?
?
The passage teaches that the good deed taken as compensation from an oppressor is not multiplied. Reflect on any grievances you hold against others. Are you willing to forgive and let go of seeking compensation, so that your good deeds can be multiplied instead? What step will you take to release that grudge?
?
If you truly believed that every sincere action could be multiplied at least tenfold, how would your daily life change? Name one small act of goodness you have been neglecting and commit to doing it today with conscious sincerity, trusting in Allah's promise of multiplication.

Expiation of Minor Sins Through Avoiding Major Sins
Defines 'avoiding major sins' and quotes Quran for expiation of minor sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that minor sins are expiated by avoiding major sins, and that 'avoiding' includes repentance after committing them.

Core Idea
Avoiding major sins means repenting from them, not just never sinning.

This lesson introduces a key theological principle: minor sins are forgiven when a person avoids major sins. But the term 'avoiding' requires careful explanation. The author clarifies that it includes repentance after falling into a major sin, not merely abstaining from them altogether. We'll also see the Quranic basis for this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on avoiding major sins
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author defines 'avoiding major sins' as encompassing repentance after committing them.
The passage also includes a Quranic verse as evidence.
2
Understand the two possible meanings of 'avoiding'
The text explicitly rejects the idea that 'avoiding' means never committing a major sin. Instead, it includes repentance. Think about why this distinction matters for a believer who may have fallen into sin.
3
Reflect on the Quranic proof
The verse from Surah an-Nisa (4:31) is cited. Notice how it directly links avoidance of major sins with expiation of misdeeds (minor sins). Consider the implications for daily life.
This verse is a primary evidence for the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباجتناب للكبائر تغفر صغائر وجا الوضو يكفر. قوله: (وباجتناب للكبائر) بسكون الراء لأنه رجز، والمراد باجتناب الكبائر ما يعم التوبة منها بعد فعلها، لا ما يخص عدم ارتكابها بالمرة بخلاف التلبس بها من غير توبة، والكبائر هي الذنوب العظيمة من حيث المؤاخذة بها. وقوله: (تغفر صغائر) أي: تكفر الذنوب الصغائر، قال تعالى: {إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ} [النساء: 31]؛ أي: الصغائر، وقال: ما من عبد يؤدي الصلوات الخمس ويصوم رمضان ويجتنب الكبائر السبع إلا فتحت له ثمانية أبواب الجنة يوم القيامة، حتى إنها لتصفق - أي: يضرب بعضها بعضاً من خلوصها - فلا يدخلها أحد حتى يدخلها. والسبع ليست بقيد بل غيرها كذلك والمراد بها الموبقات السبع؛ وهي: الشرك بالله، والسحر، وقتل النفس بغير حق، وأكل مال اليتيم، وأكل الربا، والتولي يوم الزحف، وقذف المحصنات الغافلات. وفي حديث آخر: الصلوات الخمس والجمعة إلى الجمعة، ورمضان إلى رمضان مكفرات لما بينهن إذا اجتنبت الكبائر.
And by avoiding the major sins, minor sins are forgiven, and wudu has come as expiation. His saying: (And by avoiding the major sins) with sukun on the ra because it is rajaz, and what is meant by avoiding the major sins is what includes repentance from them after committing them, not specifically not committing them at all contrary to engaging in them without repentance. And major sins are the great sins in terms of being held accountable. And his saying: (minor sins are forgiven) meaning the minor sins are expiated. He said, the Most High: {If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds} [an-Nisa: 31]; meaning the minor sins. And he said: There is no servant who performs the five prayers, fasts Ramadan, and avoids the seven major sins but that eight doors of Paradise are opened for him on the Day of Resurrection, such that they clap - meaning they strike each other from emptiness - so no one enters until he enters. And the seven are not a restriction but others are likewise, and what is meant by them are the seven destructive sins: associating partners with Allah, magic, killing a soul without right, consuming the orphan's wealth, consuming usury, turning back on the day of battle, and slandering chaste, heedless women. And in another hadith: The five prayers, and Friday to Friday, and Ramadan to Ramadan are expiations for what is between them if the major sins are avoided.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage explains the expiation of minor sins through avoiding major sins. It first clarifies that 'avoiding' includes repentance after committing the sin, not merely never committing it. Major sins are defined as those for which one is severely accountable. It quotes the Quranic verse (an-Nisa: 31) that avoiding major sins leads to expiation of minor sins. Then it cites a hadith about the seven destructive sins (al-mubiqat) that open eight doors of Paradise, and lists them: shirk, magic, murder, consuming orphan's wealth, usury, fleeing from battle, and slandering chaste women. It notes that the number seven is not restrictive; other major sins also apply. Another hadith adds that prayers, Friday, and Ramadan expiate sins between them provided major sins are avoided. This supports the condition that expiation of minor sins is contingent on avoiding major sins.

Key Terms
كبائر
صغائر
تكفير
اجتناب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that avoiding major sins means never committing them at all, and that falling into a major sin nullifies any chance of expiation.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that avoiding includes repentance after committing, so even if one falls into a major sin, repentance restores the condition for expiation of minor sins.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is included in 'avoiding' major sins?
A) Never committing them at all
B) Repenting after committing them
C) Only avoiding them before committing
D) Not thinking about them

Key Takeaway
Minor sins are expiated by avoiding major sins, where avoidance includes repentance after committing them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a minor sin you habitually commit, like a sharp tongue or a wasteful glance. According to this lesson, what condition must be met for that sin to be erased? Is there a major sin you are clinging to that might be blocking that forgiveness? What will you do about it today?
?
You may think you have avoided major sins because you have never committed the 'big ones'. But the passage says avoiding includes repenting if you have committed them. Is there a major sin in your past that you have not fully repented from? What would sincere repentance look like for you right now?
?
The promise of expiation for minor sins is conditional on avoiding major sins. Does this conditional nature make you feel secure or anxious? Are you relying on God's mercy while neglecting the requirement to actively avoid and repent from major sins? How will you adjust your priorities starting this week?

The Seven Destructive Sins and the Condition of Avoidance
Lists seven major sins and states condition for worship to expiate sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven major sins (al-mubiqat) and that regular acts of worship expiate sins only when major sins are avoided.

Core Idea
Avoiding the seven destructive sins unlocks the expiatory power of worship.

This lesson presents a famous hadith listing seven major sins that are particularly destructive, known as al-mubiqat. It also highlights that while prayers, Friday, and Ramadan expiate sins, this is conditional on avoiding major sins. The number seven is not restrictive; any major sin requires avoidance for expiation.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of seven destructive sins
Read the Arabic passage listing the seven sins: shirk, magic, murder, consuming orphan's wealth, usury, fleeing from battle, and slandering chaste women. Understand each sin's gravity.
These are from a well-known hadith.
2
Note that the number seven is not restrictive
The text explicitly says 'the seven are not a restriction but others are likewise'. So avoiding any major sin is required, not just these seven.
3
Understand the condition for expiation through worship
Another hadith mentions that the five daily prayers, Friday to Friday, and Ramadan to Ramadan are expiations for sins between them, but only if major sins are avoided. This reinforces the principle that avoidance of major sins is essential.
This shows the centrality of avoiding major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Disagreement on the Certainty of Expiation for Avoiding Major Sins
States the scholarly disagreement on certainty of expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement over whether expiation from avoiding major sins is certain or probable, and why the correct view is that it is probable.

Core Idea
Expiation from avoiding major sins is probable, not guaranteed.

Theologians agree that avoiding major sins leads to expiation of minor sins, but they disagree on the nature of this expiation: is it a certain outcome or merely probable? This brief passage presents the two positions and affirms the correct one. As you read, notice which groups hold each view and why the author sides with the theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the key terms 'قطعي' (certain) and 'ظني' (probable).
The passage is short but packed with doctrinal information.
2
Identify the two views
The passage states two opinions: one group holds expiation is certain, another holds it is probable. Identify which scholars belong to each group.
The first group includes jurists, hadith scholars, and Mu'tazila; the second group is the imams of kalam.
3
Understand why the correct view is probable
The author affirms the second view as correct. Reflect on why expiation cannot be guaranteed: it depends on Allah's will and mercy, not on human action alone.
This connects to broader Sunni theology that emphasizes divine prerogative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اتفقوا على ترتب التكفير على الاجتناب، ثم اختلفوا : هل هو قطعي أو ظني؟ فذهب جماعة من الفقهاء والمحدثين والمعتزلة إلى الأول. وذهب أئمة الكلام إلى الثاني، وهو الحق.

Commentary
This passage presents a theological disagreement: while all agree that expiation of sins is contingent on avoiding major sins, they differ on whether this expiation is certain (qat'i) or merely probable (zanni). The first view is held by some jurists, hadith scholars, and the Mu'tazila. The second view, that it is probable, is held by the imams of kalam (theologians), and the commentator affirms this as the correct position.

Wudu Expiation of Minor Sins
Explains that wudu expiates minor sins, supporting the author's verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that wudu itself expiates minor sins based on prophetic hadiths, and that this expiation does not depend on prayer.

Core Idea
Wudu alone expiates minor sins, as confirmed by multiple hadiths.

In this lesson, we examine the author's claim that wudu expiates minor sins. The passage draws on several hadiths from the Prophet (peace be upon him) to establish that wudu itself brings forgiveness, and that prayer, while encouraged, is not a condition for expiation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms 'وضوء' and 'تكفير'. Then read the translation to get the meaning.
The passage explains a line from the poem.
2
Understand the hadith evidence
Identify the three hadiths quoted. Note that each one shows that wudu brings forgiveness of sins, sometimes with prayer mentioned but not as a condition.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يتوضأ رجل مسلم فيحسن الوضوء...'
3
Grasp the broader implication
The author concludes that expiation of minor sins is not limited to avoiding major sins; wudu, prayers, fasting, and Hajj also expiate. This expands the means of forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وجا الوضو) بالقصر للوزن. وقوله: (يكفر) أي: الصغائر، ومراد المصنف أنه جاء في السنة أن الوضوء يكفر الذنوب؛ ففي الحديث عن عثمان بن عفان قال: سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: «لا يسبع أحدٌ الوضوء إلا غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه وما تأخر»، وفي الحديث أيضاً: «من توضأ نحو وضوئي هذا، ثم قام فركع ركعتين لا يحدث فيهما نفسه؛ يعني بسوء .. غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه»، وفي رواية: «لا يتوضأ رجلٌ مسلمٌ فيحسن الوضوء فيصلي صلاة إلا غفر له ما بينها وبين الصلاة التي تليها»، وذكر الصلاة في هذين الحديثين للترغيب في سنة الوضوء ليزيد ثوابه وإلا فالتكفير لا يتوقف على الصلاة، كما أخرجه أحمد مرفوعاً: «الوضوء يكفر ما قبله، ثم تصير الصلاة نافلة». وأشار المصنف بذلك إلى أنه لا ينحصر تكفير الصغائر في اجتناب الكبائر، بل الوضوء يكفرها أيضاً، وكذلك الصلوات الخمس، وكذلك صوم رمضان، وكذا الحج المبرور.
His saying: (And wudu has come) with shortening for the meter. And his saying: (it expiates) i.e., minor sins. And what the author intends is that it has come in the Sunnah that wudu expiates sins. In the hadith from Uthman ibn Affan, he said: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'No one performs wudu [imperfectly?] except that his past and future sins are forgiven him.' And in another hadith: 'Whoever performs wudu like this wudu of mine, then stands and prays two rak'ahs not speaking to himself in them – meaning about evil – his past sins are forgiven him.' And in a narration: 'No Muslim man performs wudu and does it well, then prays a prayer, except that what is between it and the prayer that follows it is forgiven him.' And mentioning prayer in these two hadiths is to encourage the sunna of wudu to increase his reward; otherwise expiation does not depend on prayer, as Ahmad narrated it marfu': 'Wudu expiates what came before it, then the prayer becomes supererogatory.' And the author indicated by that that expiation of minor sins is not limited to avoiding major sins, rather wudu also expiates them, and likewise the five prayers, and likewise fasting Ramadan, and likewise the accepted Hajj.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage explains the author's poetic line 'and wudu has come as expiation'. It clarifies that wudu expiates minor sins based on multiple hadiths. The first hadith from Uthman states that performing wudu properly forgives past and future sins. Another hadith adds that wudu followed by two rak'ahs without distraction forgives past sins. A third hadith says that wudu and prayer between two prayers expiate sins. The commentary notes that prayer is mentioned in these hadiths to encourage the sunna of wudu, but expiation does not depend on prayer; a hadith from Ahmad shows wudu alone expiates, and prayer then becomes supererogatory. The author concludes that expiation of minor sins is not confined to avoiding major sins; it also occurs through wudu, prayers, fasting Ramadan, and an accepted Hajj.

The Obligation of Believing in the Taking of the Records
Al-Ajhuri explains the obligation, definition, hadith evidence, and answers an objection about the plural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation of believing in the taking of the records, the nature of these records, and how the apparent plural in the poem is reconciled with the tradition that each person receives one record.

Core Idea
Every accountable person will receive a single record of deeds, compiled from their daily records.

This lesson focuses on a specific eschatological belief: that on the Day of Resurrection, each person will be given a record of their deeds. The poet declares this obligatory, and the commentator defends it with scriptural evidence and addresses a potential confusion about the plural term 'suhuf.' Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analysing. Notice the structure: the verse line, then commentary explaining the obligation, definition, hadith, and objection.
The passage is a commentary on the poetic line.
2
Understand the obligation
The obligation is established by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus; denial is kufr. Note the definition of suhuf as the books in which angels recorded deeds.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith about daily records being bright or dark based on seeking forgiveness shows the importance of istighfar and implies that multiple daily records are combined into one final record.
4
Resolve the plural objection
The objection asks why the poet used the plural 'suhuf' if each person gets one record. The answer is that it is a distributive plural: the plural matches the plural of servants, meaning each servant gets one record.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ وجوبُ اعتقادِ أخذ العبادِ صحائف أعمالهم] ١٠٤ - وَوَاجِبٌ أَخْذُ العِبَادِ الصُّحُفا كَمَا مِنَ القُرْآنِ نَمَّا عُرِفَا قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ أَخْذُ العِبَادِ الصُّحُفَا) (واجب) خبر مقدَّم، وأخذ العباد) مبتدأ مؤخر، والأصل: وأخذ العباد الصحفا واجب؛ أي: سمعاً لوروده كتاباً وسنة ولانعقاد الإجماع عليه فيجب الإيمان به، ومن أنكره كَفَر ؛ والمراد من الصحف: الكتب التي كتبت فيها الملائكة ما فعله العباد في الدنيا، والأحاديث صريحة الظواهر في أن كل مكلَّف له صحيفةٌ واحدة يوم القيامة، مع أنها كانت متعددة في الدنيا؛ كما يدل عليه حديث: «ما من مؤمن إلا وله كل يوم صحيفة، فإذا طويت وليس فيها استغفار طويت وهي سوداء مظلمة، وإذا طويت وفيها استغفار طويت ولها نور يتلألأ»، وقد اختلف فقيل : توصل صحف الأيام والليالي، وقيل: ينسخ ما في جميعها في صحيفة واحدة. فإن قيل : إذا كان كلُّ مكلف له صحيفةٌ واحدةٌ يوم القيامة .. فلم جمعها المصنف؟ أجيب بأنه جمعها في مقابلة جمع العباد فهو من مقابلة الجمع بالجمع، فتقسم الآحاد على الآحاد وظواهر الآيات والأحاديث شاهدة بعمومه لجميع الأمم، نعم الأنبياء لا يأخذون ،صحفاً، وكذا الملائكة لعصمتهم، ومن يدخل الجنة بغير حساب، ورئيسهم أبو بكر الصديق رضي الله تعالى عنه، ولم يذكر المصنف من يدفع الصحف للعباد.
[The obligation of believing in the taking of the records of deeds by the servants] 104 - And obligatory is the taking of the records by the servants, as is known from the Quran. His saying: 'And obligatory is the taking of the records by the servants' – (obligatory) is a predicate preceding, and 'the taking of the records by the servants' is a subject delayed; the original is: 'the taking of the records by the servants is obligatory'; meaning: it is obligatory due to its transmission in the Book and the Sunnah and the consensus upon it, so belief in it is obligatory, and whoever denies it disbelieves. What is meant by the records are the books in which the angels wrote what the servants did in the world. The hadiths are explicit in their indications that every legally responsible person has one record on the Day of Resurrection, even though they were multiple in the world; as indicated by the hadith: 'There is no believer except that every day he has a record; if it is folded and there is no seeking of forgiveness in it, it is folded while it is black and dark; and if it is folded and there is seeking of forgiveness in it, it is folded and has a light that glistens.' And there is disagreement: it is said that the records of days and nights are connected; and it is said that what is in all of them is copied into one record. If it is said: If every legally responsible person has one record on the Day of Resurrection, why did the author (of the poem) use the plural? The answer is: he used the plural to correspond to the plurality of servants, so it is a case of matching plural with plural; thus the singulars are distributed among the singulars. The apparent meanings of the verses and hadiths testify to its generality for all nations. Yes, the prophets do not take records, likewise the angels because of their infallibility, and those who enter Paradise without reckoning – their chief is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him. And the author did not mention who gives the records to the servants.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation of believing that the servants will take their records of deeds on the Day of Resurrection. It begins by quoting the poetic line that states this obligation. The commentator explains the grammar: 'obligatory' is a predicate and 'the taking of the records by the servants' is the subject. The obligation is established by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus; denial is disbelief. Then he defines 'suhuf' as the books in which angels recorded deeds. He notes that hadiths indicate that each accountable person will have one record on Qiyamah, even though they had multiple records in the world. He provides a hadith about daily records becoming bright or dark based on seeking forgiveness. There is disagreement on how the multiple records are combined: either connected or copied into one. Then he addresses an objection: since each person gets one record, why did the poet use the plural 'suhuf'? The answer is that it is a case of distributing plurals: the plural of records corresponds to the plural of servants. The verses and hadiths apply to all nations. Then he mentions exceptions: prophets, angels, and those who enter Paradise without reckoning (headed by Abu Bakr) do not take records. He notes that the poet did not specify who gives the records.

Key Terms
صحف
صحيفة
أخذ الصحف
الإيمان بالصحف
الكتب
الإجماع
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the poet used the plural 'suhuf', each person receives multiple records on the Day of Resurrection.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it is a distributive plural: the plural matches the plural of servants, meaning each servant receives one record.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to someone who denies the obligation of believing in the taking of the records?
A) He is a sinner
B) He is an innovator
C) He commits disbelief
D) He is excused

Key Takeaway
Belief in the taking of the records is obligatory and supported by revelation; the plural term is a distributive plural, and certain individuals (prophets, angels, etc.) are exempt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The hadith mentions that a record folded without istighfar becomes dark and gloomy. Have you sincerely asked for forgiveness today? If not, what is holding you back from turning to Allah with a broken heart?
?
You will face a single summary of your entire life. If you had to write its title now, what would it be? Are you satisfied with that title, or do you need to rewrite your story starting this moment?

Reconciling Reports on the Distribution of Records
Al-Ajhuri reconciles two reports on record distribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars reconcile apparent contradictions between scriptural reports about the distribution of records on Judgment Day.

Core Idea
Two reports, one sequence: wind first, angels second.

In this lesson, we examine two reports about how the records of deeds are distributed on the Day of Judgment. One mentions a wind from beneath the Throne, another mentions angels giving the books directly. Al-Ajhuri presents the reconciliation that preserves both reports as describing stages of the same event.

Guided Path
1
Read the two reports
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the two reports that appear contradictory: one about a wind distributing records, another about angels giving books directly. The author then introduces the reconciliation.
The passage begins with 'وقد ورد أن الريح تطيرها...' and then 'وورد أيضاً أن كل أحدٍ يُدعى...'
2
Identify the contradiction
The two reports seem to describe different mechanisms for distributing records. Ask yourself: How can both be true? The author states there is a contradiction ('فحصل التعارض بين الروايتين').
3
Understand the reconciliation
The reconciliation is presented in the phrase 'وجمع بينهما بأن الريح تُطيِّرُها أولاً... ثم تناديهم الملائكة فتأخذها من أعناقهم وتعطيها لهم في أيديهم'. This shows a sequence: wind first attaches records to necks, then angels take and hand them over.
This resolves the contradiction by showing both reports are true at different moments.
4
Note the final outcome
The passage ends by describing how the believer and disbeliever receive their books: the believer takes it in his right hand, the disbeliever in his left from behind. This reinforces the complete picture.
The text cuts off at 'وأما المؤمن الفاسق' but we have enough to see the sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ورد أن الريح تطيرها من خزانة تحت العرش، فلا تُخطئ صحيفةٌ عنق صاحبها (۱) ، وورد أيضاً أن كل أحدٍ يُدعى فيُعطى كتابه (۳) ، فحصل التعارض بين الروايتين، وجمع بينهما بأن الريح تُطيِّرُها أولاً من الخزانة فتتعلق كل صحيفة بعنق صاحبها، ثم تناديهم الملائكة فتأخذها من أعناقهم وتعطيها لهم في أيديهم؛ فالمؤمن المطيع يأخذ كتابه بيمينه والكافر يأخذه بشماله من وراء ظهره، وأما المؤمن الفاسق (۱) أورده القرطبي في التذكرة بأحوال الموتى وأمور الآخرة (١/ ٦١٥). وقال السيوطي في «البدور السافرة في أحوال الآخرة» (۱۰۸): جاء عن أنس مالك بن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «الكتب كلها تحت العرش، فإذا كان الموقف بعث الله ريحاً فتطيرها بالأيمان والشمائل، أول خط فيها أقرأ كتبكَ كَفَى بِنَفْسِك اليوم عليك حبيبا [الإسراء: ١٤]، أخرجه الترمذي . أخرج العقيلي (٤٦٦/٤) من طريق يغنم بن سالم بن قنبر عن أنس بن مالك مرفوعاً : الكتب كلها تحت العرش، فإذا كان الموقف بعث الله ريحاً فتطيرها بالأيمان والشمائل، أول خط فيها أقرأ كتَبَكَ كَفَى بِنَفْسِكَ الْيَوْمَ عَلَيْكَ حَسِيبًا [الإسراء : ١٤] . (۲) أخرج البخاري (٢٤٤١) : إن الله يُدني المؤمن فيضع عليه كنفه ويستره، فيقول: أتعرف ذنب كذا، أتعرف ذنب كذا؟ فيقول : نعم أي رب، حتى إذا قرره بذنوبه، ورأى في نفسه أنه هلك، قال : سترتها عليك في الدنيا، وأنا أغفرها لك اليوم، فيعطى كتاب حسناته، وأما الكافر والمنافقون فيقول الأشهاد : هَؤُلَاءِ الَّذِينَ كَذَبُوا عَلَى رَبِّهِمْ أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللَّهِ عَلَى الظَّلِمِينَ) [هود : ١٨]، وينحوه (٤٦٨٥)، ومسلم (٢٧٦٨).
And it has been reported that the wind flies them from a treasury beneath the Throne, and no record misses the neck of its owner (1). And it has also been reported that every person is called and given his book (3). Thus there is a contradiction between the two reports. They are reconciled by saying: the wind first flies them from the treasury, so each record attaches to the neck of its owner, then the angels call them and take them from their necks and give them into their hands. Commentary
This passage addresses two reports that appear contradictory. One report says that a wind from under the Throne distributes records, each attaching to its owner's neck. Another report says that each person is called and given his book directly. The contradiction is resolved by a sequence: first the wind flies the records from the treasury and each record attaches to its owner's neck; then the angels call the people, take the records from their necks, and hand them to their hands. The believer receives his book in his right hand, the disbeliever in his left from behind. The passage ends with mention of the disobedient believer, but the text cuts off. The footnotes provide sources for the reports.

The Reality of Reading the Books of Deeds
Commentator discusses the reality of reading the Books of Deeds, arguing it is literal and describing the physical books.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly debate on whether the reading of the Book of Deeds on Judgment Day is literal or metaphorical, and the physical description of the books for believers and disbelievers.

Core Idea
The reading of the Book of Deeds is literally real, not metaphorical.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key question in Islamic eschatology: is the 'reading' of the Books of Deeds on Judgment Day a literal event or a metaphor for knowing one's deeds? After quoting the Quranic verses, he argues for the preponderant view that it is real and describes the physical state of the books. This lesson unpacks his argument and the supporting details.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic evidence
The commentator begins by quoting verses 84:7-12. Understand the context of these verses and how they describe the giving of the book.
2
Identify the main issue
The passage presents two views: that the reading is literal (haqiqa) or metaphorical (majaz). Note that the commentator states the preponderant view is literal.
3
Examine the description of the books
The believer's book is white with white writing, and the disbeliever's book is black with black writing. Reading them affects the face color.
This is a physical description, not just symbolic.
4
Note the scholarly disagreement and reconciliation
Sheikh Abd al-Salam said only the first line determines the face color, but the commentator reconciles by saying the rest is similar.
Reflect on why this detail matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكقوله تعالى: ﴿فَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كِتَابَهُ يَمِينِهِ فَسَوْفَ يُحَاسَبُ حِسَابًا يَسِيرًا وَيَنقَلِبُ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ مَسْرُورًا وَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كِتَابَهُ وَرَاءَ ظَهْرِهِ فَسَوْفَ يَدْعُو ثُبُورًا وَيَصْلَى سَعِيرًا﴾ [الانشقاق: 7-12]. وظاهر كلامهم أن القراءة حقيقةٌ، وهو الراجح، وقيل: مجاز عن علم كل أحدٍ بما له وعليه، ويقرأ كل أحد كتابه، ولو كان أُمِّيًّا، لكن من الآخذين من لم يقرأ كتابه ذهولاً ودهشةً لاشتماله على القبائح. والمؤمن يأتيه كتابه أبيض بكتابة بيضاء، ويأخذه بيمينه فيقرأه فيبيض وجهه، والكافر يأتيه كتابه أسود بكتابة سوداء، فيقرؤه فيسود وجهه، كما ذكره المصنف في «كبيره». والذي ذكره الشيخ عبد السلام: أنَّ أول سطر من صحيفة المؤمن أبيضُ فإذا قرأه ابيض وجههُ، والكافر بضد ذلك. انتهى. ويمكن ترجيع كلامه لكلام والده بأن يُقال: لا مفهوم لقوله: أول سطر، بل مثله الباقي، فتأمل.
And as His statement the Exalted: [verse 84:7-12]. And the apparent meaning of their speech is that the reading is real, and it is the preponderant view, and it was said: it is a metaphor for the knowledge of each one of what is for him and against him, and each one reads his book, even if he is illiterate, but among those who take it there is he who does not read his book due to distraction and bewilderment because it contains evil deeds. And the believer is brought his book white with white writing, and he takes it with his right hand and reads it, and his face becomes white; and the disbeliever is brought his book black with black writing, and he reads it, and his face becomes black, as the author mentioned in his 'Kabir'. And what Sheikh Abd al-Salam mentioned: that the first line of the believer's page is white, so when he reads it his face becomes white, and the disbeliever is the opposite. End. And it is possible to reconcile his statement with his father's statement by saying: there is no concept to his saying 'first line', rather the rest is like it, so reflect.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage explains the reality of the reading of the books of deeds on Judgment Day. The commentator begins by quoting Quran 84:7-12, which describes the reception of the book in the right hand or behind the back. He then states that the apparent meaning of the scholars is that the reading is real (ḥaqīqa), and this is the preponderant view. Some said it is metaphorical (majāz), meaning that each person knows what he has done, but the commentator prefers the literal reading. He notes that everyone will read his own book, even if illiterate, though some may be too distracted to read it due to its content. The passage then describes the physical state of the books: the believer's book is white with white writing, and when read, his face becomes white; the disbeliever's book is black with black writing, and his face becomes black. The commentator references the author's larger work (al-Kabīr) and then quotes Sheikh Abd al-Salam, who said that only the first line determines the color of the face. The commentator reconciles this difference by saying that there is no exclusive concept to 'first line'—the rest of the lines are similar. This lesson guards against interpreting the reading as merely metaphorical and affirms the physical reality of the book and its effects.

Key Terms
القراءة حقيقة
مجاز
صحيفة المؤمن
صحيفة الكافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The reading of the Books of Deeds is only a metaphor for inner knowledge, not a literal event.
Correction: The preponderant view is that it is a literal, real reading with physical effects.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the preponderant view regarding the reading of the books of deeds?
A) It is metaphorical (majāz)
B) It is real (ḥaqīqa)
C) It is only for the literate
D) It does not happen for the disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The reading of the Book of Deeds on Judgment Day is a literal, real event, and the physical state of the book reflects the person's faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage says some are so overwhelmed by their evil deeds that they cannot read their book. Is there any sin in your life that you are avoiding confronting? What would it take for you to read it now and turn to Allah?
?
The book is already written. How does that reality change what you will do in the next hour? Will you choose an action that whitens your book or blackens it?

Obligation to Believe in the Weighing and the Scale
Commentator establishes obligation and describes the scale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in the weighing of deeds and the scale is an obligatory matter transmitted from revelation, and you will learn the description of the scale according to the predominant view.

Core Idea
The scale is one, larger than the heavens and earth, and attended by angels.

This lesson introduces the obligation to believe in the weighing of deeds and the scale, linking it to the previous discussion of taking the scrolls. The commentator explains that just as taking the scrolls is obligatory based on transmitted knowledge, so too is belief in the weighing. He then describes the scale in vivid detail, noting that it is a single scale with a beam, pillar, and two pans that are vast beyond imagination, and that angels Jibril and Mika'il are involved in its operation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how the commentator connects this topic to the previous one (taking the scrolls).
2
Identify the key claim
The commentator states that belief in the weighing and scale is obligatory (wājib). Find the phrase that indicates this.
The phrase 'وجوب الإيمان' (obligation of belief) appears at the beginning.
3
Visualize the scale
The scale is described with specific parts: a beam (qaṣaba), a pillar (ʿamūd), and two pans (kiffatān). Each pan is wider than the layers of the heavens and earth. Try to form a mental picture.
The description emphasizes the scale's immense size.
4
Note the angels' roles
Jibril is looking at the tongue of the scale, and Mika'il is in charge of it. Reflect on why these particular angels are assigned.
This shows the importance of the event.
5
Consider the alternative view
The commentator mentions an alternative opinion that each person has multiple scales for different types of deeds. Compare this with the predominant view of one scale.
The phrase 'وقيل' (and it was said) introduces the alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوبُ الإيمان بالوزن والميزان. ومِثْلُ هَذَا الوَزْنُ والمِيزَانُ فَتُوزَنُ الكُتُبُ أَوِ الأَعْيانُ. قوله: ومِثْلُ هَذَا الوَزْنُ والمِيزَانُ أي: ومثلُ أخذ العباد الصحف في الوجوب السمعي وزن أعمال العباد والميزان، وهو ميزان واحد على الراجح، له قصبةٌ وعمود وكِفَّتان، كلُّ واحدة منهما أوسع من طباق السموات والأرض، وجبريل آخذ ناظر إلى لسانه، وميكائيل أمين عليه(١) ، ومحله بعد الحساب. وقيل: لكلِّ عامل موازين يُوزن بكلِّ منها صنف من عمله. ويدل على الوزن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْوَزْنُ يَوْمَئِذٍ الْحَقُّ﴾ [الأعراف: ٨]، وعلى الميزان قوله تعالى: ﴿وَنَضَعُ الْمَوَازِينَ الْقِسْطَ لِيَوْمِ الْقِيَامَةِ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٤٧]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن ثَقُلَتْ مَوَازِينُهُ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْمُفْلِحُونَ وَمَنْ خَفَّتْ مَوَازِينُهُ فَأُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ خَسِرُوا أَنفُسَهُمْ﴾ [الأعراف: ٨-٩]. وخِفَّة الموزون وثقله على صورته (١) في الدنيا، وقيل: على عكس صورته في الدنيا، فالثقيلُ يصعد إلى أعلى والخفيف ينزل إلى أسفل، لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْعَمَلُ الصَّالِحُ يَرْفَعُهُ﴾ [فاطر: ١٠]. والجمعُ فيما ذكر للتعظيم على المشهور من أنه ميزانٌ لجميع الأمم ولجميع الأعمال؛ وقد بلغت أحاديثه مبلغ التواتر فيجب الإيمان به ونُمْسِكُ عن تعيين حقيقته.
The obligation of belief in the weight and the scale. And like this is the weight and the scale, so the books or the deeds are weighed. His statement: 'And like this is the weight and the scale' means: and like the taking of the servants' scrolls in the obligatory (ruling) by transmission is the weighing of the servants' deeds and the scale. It is one scale according to the preponderant view. It has a beam, a pillar, and two pans, each of which is wider than the layers of the heavens and the earth. Jibril (Gabriel) is taking, looking at its tongue, and Mika'il (Michael) is in charge of it. Its place is after the reckoning. And it was said: for every worker there are scales with which each type of his deed is weighed. Evidence for the weight: His statement the Exalted: 'And the weight on that day is the truth' [7:8]; and for the scale: 'And We shall set up the scales of justice for the Day of Resurrection' [21:47]; and His statement: 'Then those whose scales are heavy, they are the successful, and those whose scales are light, they are the ones who have lost their souls' [7:8-9]. The lightness and heaviness of the weighed thing is according to its form in this world, and it was said: contrary to its form in this world, so the heavy ascends upward and the light descends downward, due to His statement: 'And righteous deeds raise it' [35:10]. The plural in what is mentioned is for magnification according to the well-known view that it is one scale for all nations and for all deeds. Its hadiths have reached the level of tawatur (mass transmission), so belief in it is obligatory, and we refrain from specifying its reality.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage establishes the doctrinal obligation to believe in the weighing (wazn) and the scale (mīzān) of deeds on Judgment Day. It begins by linking this obligation to the previous discussion about taking the scrolls—both are matters of samʿiyyāt (transmitted knowledge). The commentator explains that the scale is a single physical scale, according to the preponderant view, with a beam, pillar, and two pans, each larger than the heavens and earth. Angels Jibril and Mika'il are involved in its operation. It takes place after the reckoning. An alternative view holds that each person has multiple scales for different types of deeds. Scriptural proofs from Qur'an 7:8, 21:47, and 7:8-9 are cited. The passage then discusses how the weight and lightness relate to the deeds' forms—either as they are in the world or reversed, with the heavy ascending and the light descending, supported by Qur'an 35:10. The plural 'balances' (mawāzīn) in the Qur'an is for magnification, not multiplicity. The hadiths on this topic are mutawātir, making belief obligatory, yet we do not specify the exact reality (ḥaqīqa).

Key Terms
الوزن
الميزان
الحساب
الموازين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the weighing is metaphorical or that there are many separate scales.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the scale is one physical entity (on the preponderant view) and that belief in it is obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant view mentioned in the passage, how many scales are there?
A) One scale for all nations
B) Each person has their own scale
C) Each deed has its own scale
D) There is no scale, only weights

Key Takeaway
Belief in the weighing and the scale is an obligatory part of Islamic creed, and the scale is described as one massive physical entity with angels attending it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that your deeds will be weighed on a scale vast enough to hold the heavens and earth, what specific deed are you most afraid to see placed on that scale, and what will you change today to tip the balance toward mercy?
?
If Jibril himself watches the tongue of the scale, how should that awareness shape your next conversation, your next private thought, your next moment of anger?
?
The passage mentions an alternative view that each type of deed has its own scale. Which area of your life—prayer, charity, honesty, or something else—would you be most anxious about if that were true? What is one concrete step you will take this week to improve that scale?

Scriptural Evidence and Nature of Weighing
Commentator provides scriptural evidence and explains nature of weighing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qur'anic evidence for the weighing and scale, how deeds are weighed, and why the plural 'balances' is used for a single scale.

Core Idea
Qur'anic verses prove the weighing; deeds are weighed as they are or reversed; 'balances' is for magnification.

This lesson presents the scriptural proofs for the weighing and the scale, drawing on three Qur'anic verses. It then discusses how the lightness or heaviness of deeds relates to their form in this world or the opposite, and explains that the plural form 'mawāzīn' in the Qur'an is a linguistic device for magnifying a single scale. The commentator concludes that the hadiths on this topic are mutawātir, making belief obligatory while avoiding speculation about its exact reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage, paying attention to the Qur'anic verses quoted. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
2
Identify the three verses
The commentator cites three verses: one for the concept of weight (wazn), and two for the scale (mīzān). Locate each verse in the passage.
The verses are from Surah al-A'raf and Surah al-Anbiya'.
3
Understand the two opinions on weight
The passage presents two views: the lightness/heaviness is according to the deed's form in this world, or it is the opposite (heavy ascends, light descends). Consider which is supported by the quoted verse from Surah Fatir.
The verse 'wa-l-ʿamalu ṣ-ṣāliḥu yarfaʿuh' suggests the heavy (good deed) rises.
4
Explain the plural 'mawāzīn'
The commentator says the plural is for magnification (taʿẓīm), not multiplicity. Understand why this linguistic point matters for correct belief.
This resolves an apparent contradiction with the one scale view.
5
Conclude with the obligatory nature
The hadiths have reached tawatur, so belief is obligatory, yet we refrain from specifying the exact reality (ḥaqīqa).
This shows balance between conviction and epistemic humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوبُ الإيمان بالوزن والميزان. ومِثْلُ هَذَا الوَزْنُ والمِيزَانُ فَتُوزَنُ الكُتُبُ أَوِ الأَعْيانُ. قوله: ومِثْلُ هَذَا الوَزْنُ والمِيزَانُ أي: ومثلُ أخذ العباد الصحف في الوجوب السمعي وزن أعمال العباد والميزان، وهو ميزان واحد على الراجح، له قصبةٌ وعمود وكِفَّتان، كلُّ واحدة منهما أوسع من طباق السموات والأرض، وجبريل آخذ ناظر إلى لسانه، وميكائيل أمين عليه(١) ، ومحله بعد الحساب. وقيل: لكلِّ عامل موازين يُوزن بكلِّ منها صنف من عمله. ويدل على الوزن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْوَزْنُ يَوْمَئِذٍ الْحَقُّ﴾ [الأعراف: ٨]، وعلى الميزان قوله تعالى: ﴿وَنَضَعُ الْمَوَازِينَ الْقِسْطَ لِيَوْمِ الْقِيَامَةِ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٤٧]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن ثَقُلَتْ مَوَازِينُهُ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْمُفْلِحُونَ وَمَنْ خَفَّتْ مَوَازِينُهُ فَأُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ خَسِرُوا أَنفُسَهُمْ﴾ [الأعراف: ٨-٩]. وخِفَّة الموزون وثقله على صورته (١) في الدنيا، وقيل: على عكس صورته في الدنيا، فالثقيلُ يصعد إلى أعلى والخفيف ينزل إلى أسفل، لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْعَمَلُ الصَّالِحُ يَرْفَعُهُ﴾ [فاطر: ١٠]. والجمعُ فيما ذكر للتعظيم على المشهور من أنه ميزانٌ لجميع الأمم ولجميع الأعمال؛ وقد بلغت أحاديثه مبلغ التواتر فيجب الإيمان به ونُمْسِكُ عن تعيين حقيقته.
The obligation of belief in the weight and the scale. And like this is the weight and the scale, so the books or the deeds are weighed. His statement: 'And like this is the weight and the scale' means: and like the taking of the servants' scrolls in the obligatory (ruling) by transmission is the weighing of the servants' deeds and the scale. It is one scale according to the preponderant view. It has a beam, a pillar, and two pans, each of which is wider than the layers of the heavens and the earth. Jibril (Gabriel) is taking, looking at its tongue, and Mika'il (Michael) is in charge of it. Its place is after the reckoning. And it was said: for every worker there are scales with which each type of his deed is weighed. Evidence for the weight: His statement the Exalted: 'And the weight on that day is the truth' [7:8]; and for the scale: 'And We shall set up the scales of justice for the Day of Resurrection' [21:47]; and His statement: 'Then those whose scales are heavy, they are the successful, and those whose scales are light, they are the ones who have lost their souls' [7:8-9]. The lightness and heaviness of the weighed thing is according to its form in this world, and it was said: contrary to its form in this world, so the heavy ascends upward and the light descends downward, due to His statement: 'And righteous deeds raise it' [35:10]. The plural in what is mentioned is for magnification according to the well-known view that it is one scale for all nations and for all deeds. Its hadiths have reached the level of tawatur (mass transmission), so belief in it is obligatory, and we refrain from specifying its reality.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage establishes the doctrinal obligation to believe in the weighing (wazn) and the scale (mīzān) of deeds on Judgment Day. It begins by linking this obligation to the previous discussion about taking the scrolls—both are matters of samʿiyyāt (transmitted knowledge). The commentator explains that the scale is a single physical scale, according to the preponderant view, with a beam, pillar, and two pans, each larger than the heavens and earth. Angels Jibril and Mika'il are involved in its operation. It takes place after the reckoning. An alternative view holds that each person has multiple scales for different types of deeds. Scriptural proofs from Qur'an 7:8, 21:47, and 7:8-9 are cited. The passage then discusses how the weight and lightness relate to the deeds' forms—either as they are in the world or reversed, with the heavy ascending and the light descending, supported by Qur'an 35:10. The plural 'balances' (mawāzīn) in the Qur'an is for magnification, not multiplicity. The hadiths on this topic are mutawātir, making belief obligatory, yet we do not specify the exact reality (ḥaqīqa).

الميزان
الموازين
تواتر

Misconception: Some might think the plural 'mawāzīn' proves multiple distinct scales, or that we can fully understand the mechanics of weighing.
Correction: The commentator explains the plural is for magnification, and we withhold specifying the exact reality.

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse does the commentator use to support the idea that the heavy (good deeds) ascend upward?
A) 7:8
B) 21:47
C) 7:8-9
D) 35:10

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an and mutawātir hadiths obligate belief in the weighing and a single scale, but we do not speculate about its exact nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says we must believe in the scale but refrain from specifying its reality. Are you content to submit to Allah's wisdom without demanding to know the details? What in your heart needs to let go of the need to understand everything?
?
You read that the hadith on this topic are mutawātir, so belief is obligatory. Have you truly accepted this unseen reality as more certain than the physical world around you? How does that conviction change your actions right now?

Answer to Objection on Weighing Disbelievers' Deeds
Objection and answer regarding how disbelievers' deeds can be weighed when they have no good deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the deeds of disbelievers are weighed on the Day of Judgment, despite their lack of valid intention.

Core Idea
Disbelievers' good deeds are weighed, but unbelief always outweighs them.

The concept of weighing deeds on the Day of Judgment raises a natural question: How can disbelievers have their deeds weighed if they have no good deeds? This lesson presents an objection and its answer, clarifying that disbelievers do perform certain good deeds, but these are outweighed by their unbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection that disbelievers have no good deeds. Notice how the objector contrasts believers and disbelievers.
2
Examine the answer
Read the answer which lists deeds of disbelievers that are valid without intention: maintaining family ties, helping people, freeing slaves. Understand that these are placed in the scale against their sins other than unbelief.
3
Understand the treatment of unbelief
The passage states that unbelief itself is not weighed because its punishment is eternal. Reflect on why weighing unbelief would be useless.
4
Consider al-Qurtubi's clarification
Read the citation from al-Qurtubi, who explicitly says these good deeds are placed in the scale, then unbelief outweighs them. This reinforces that the scale does show the disbeliever's deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: وزنُ أعمال المؤمنين وجهه ظاهرُ؛ إذ له من الحسنات ما يقابل السيئات، وأما الكفار فليس لهم حسنات حتى تقابل بها سيئاتهم. أجيب بأنه يكون منهم صلة الرحم ومواساة الناس وعتق المماليك ونحوها من الأعمال التي لا تتوقف صحتها على نيَّة، فتجعل هذه الأمور - إن صدرت منهم - في مقابلة سيئاتهم غير الكفر، أما هو فلا فائدة في وزنه؛ لأن عذابه دائم. وفي كلام القرطبي ما يُصرح بوزنه، حيث قال: فتجمع له هذه الأمور وتوضع في ميزانه؛ يعني الكافر، فيرجح الكفر بها. انتهى.
For if it is said: Weighing the deeds of the believers, its aspect is apparent, since he has good deeds to counterbalance the evil deeds. As for the disbelievers, they have no good deeds to counterbalance their evil deeds. It is answered: That there may be from them maintaining family ties, comforting people, freeing slaves, and similar deeds whose validity does not depend on intention. So these matters—if they occur from them—are placed in opposition to their evil deeds other than unbelief. As for unbelief, there is no benefit in weighing it, because its punishment is eternal. And in the speech of al-Qurtubi there is explicit mention of weighing it, where he said: 'So these matters are gathered for him and placed in his scale—meaning the disbeliever—and then unbelief outweighs them.' End.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair regarding the weighing of disbelievers' deeds. The objector argues that weighing makes sense for believers because they have good deeds to counterbalance their bad deeds. Disbelievers, however, have no good deeds, so what is the point of weighing? The answer explains that disbelievers may perform certain good deeds that do not require valid intention (niyya), such as maintaining family ties, helping people, and freeing slaves. These deeds are placed in their scale to counterbalance their sins other than unbelief. Unbelief itself is not weighed because its punishment is eternal and cannot be outweighed. The commentator cites al-Qurtubi, who explicitly states that these good deeds are gathered and placed in the scale of the disbeliever, and then unbelief tips the scale. This lesson clarifies that disbelievers can have weightable good deeds, but their unbelief remains dominant and unredeemable.

الكفار
الحسنات
السيئات
نية
صلة الرحم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disbelievers have absolutely no good deeds to weigh on the Day of Judgment.
Correction: Disbelievers do have good deeds like maintaining family ties and freeing slaves, which are weighed against their sins other than unbelief, but unbelief itself remains dominant.

Checkpoint

C) Deeds like maintaining family ties and freeing slaves
D) No deeds, because they are disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers' good deeds that do not require intention are weighed against their sins, but unbelief outweighs them due to its eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that even disbelievers can have good deeds like maintaining family ties. In your own life, do you sometimes perform kind acts without a sincere intention for Allah? How will you purify your intentions starting today?
?
Unbelief outweighs all good deeds. What is the state of your faith right now? Are there any attitudes or actions in your heart that amount to 'unbelief' and might tip your scale? What specific step will you take this week to strengthen your iman?
?
The objection in the passage questions Allah's justice, but the answer reveals His perfect wisdom. When you face situations where Allah's decree seems illogical, do you trust Him completely? Will you commit to submitting to His judgment even when you don't understand?

The Weighing: Books or Deeds? A Scholarly Debate
Scholar's differing views on whether the books of deeds or the deeds themselves are weighed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing scholarly views on what is weighed on the Day of Judgment and the evidence from the hadith of the card.

Core Idea
On Judgment Day, the books containing deeds, not the deeds themselves, are weighed according to most scholars.

This lesson addresses a key question in Islamic eschatology: what exactly is placed on the scales on the Day of Judgment? The text presents two views: that the physical books of deeds are weighed, or that the deeds themselves are weighed. The author notes the majority position and supports it with the famous hadith of the card (biṭāqa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
The passage begins by stating the disagreement: 'so the books or the deeds are weighed'. Note the two possibilities.
2
Understand the majority position
The majority of exegetes hold that the books are weighed because deeds are recorded in separate books.
3
Examine the evidence
The hadith of the card is cited as proof. Read the partial hadith and consider how it supports the view that books are weighed.
The full hadith continues, but this excerpt shows the scrolls being spread.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the debate matters for our understanding of divine justice and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فَتُوزَنُ الكُتُبُ أَوِ الأَعْيانُ) أشار بذلك إلى اختلاف العلماء في الموزون، فذهب جمهور المفسرين إلى أن الموزون الكتب التي اشتملت على أعمال العباد، بناء على أن الحسنات مميزة بكتاب والسيئات بآخر ويشهد له حديث البطاقة، بكسر الموحدة وهي ورقة صغيرة وحديثها: ما رُوي عن عبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: إن الله يستخلص رجلاً من أمتي على رؤوس الخلائق يوم القيامة، فيُنْشَرُ عليه تسعة وتسعون سِجَلا، كل سجل منها مدُّ البصر، ثم يقول: أتنكرُ من هذا شيئاً؟ أظلمك كتبتي الحافظون؟ فيقول: لا يا رَبِّ، فيقولُ: ألك عُذر؟ فيقول: لا يا رَبِّ، فيقول: ألك حسنةٌ؟ فيقول: لا يا رَبِّ، فيقول: بلى إن لك عندنا لحسنة
His statement: (So the books or the deeds are weighed). He indicated by that the difference of the scholars concerning what is weighed. The majority of exegetes hold that what is weighed are the books which contain the deeds of the servants, based on the fact that the good deeds are distinguished in one book and the evil deeds in another. Evidence for this is the hadith of the card (biṭāqa), with a kasra on the bā', which is a small paper. Its hadith: It is narrated from Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, that he said: 'Indeed Allah will single out a man from my community before the heads of mankind on the Day of Resurrection, and ninety-nine scrolls will be spread out for him, each scroll extending as far as the eye can see. Then He will say: Do you deny any of this? Have My recording angels wronged you? He will say: No, my Lord. He will say: Do you have an excuse? He will say: No, my Lord. He will say: Do you have a good deed? He will say: No, my Lord. He will say: Yes, indeed you have with Us a good deed.'
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage explains the different scholarly views on what exactly is weighed on the Day of Judgment: the physical books (kutub) containing the deeds, or the deeds themselves (aʿyān). The commentator notes that his statement 'so the books or the deeds are weighed' indicates this disagreement. The majority of exegetes (jumhūr al-mufassirīn) hold that the books are weighed, because good and evil deeds are recorded in separate books. They cite as evidence the well-known hadith of the card (ḥadīth al-biṭāqa), which describes a man from the ummah who is confronted with ninety-nine scrolls of sins but is saved by a single good deed (a card containing the shahāda). The hadith is quoted partially here, mentioning the scrolls and the dialogue. This lesson shows that the debate is not settled; both views are mentioned, with the majority favoring books.

Key Terms
الموزون
الكتب
الأعيان
حديث البطاقة
جمهور المفسرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think only deeds themselves are weighed, ignoring the possibility that the books containing them are weighed.
Correction: The majority of scholars hold that the books (kutub) are weighed, citing the hadith of the scrolls as evidence.

Checkpoint

A) The deeds themselves (aʿyān)
B) The books (kutub) containing the deeds
C) Both the books and the deeds
D) Only the good deeds

Key Takeaway
The majority of exegetes hold that the books of deeds are weighed on the Day of Judgment, supported by the hadith of the card.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Knowing that every action is recorded in a book that will be weighed, does it change how you speak about others, how you spend your time, or what you think about when alone? What specific accountability will you put in place this week to live with this awareness?
