Weighing the Deeds of Disbelievers
Objection and answer about weighing deeds of disbelievers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the weighing of deeds applies to disbelievers according to Sunni theology, including which deeds are weighed and why disbelief itself is not weighed.

Core Idea
Disbelievers' virtuous deeds are weighed against lesser sins, but not against disbelief.

This passage addresses a classic objection: if disbelievers have no good deeds, why would their deeds be weighed? The answer clarifies that they do perform certain virtuous acts that are valid without intention, and these are weighed against their non-kufr sins. Disbelief itself is not weighed because its punishment is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: weighing deeds only makes sense for believers who have both good and bad deeds. Disbelievers lack good deeds.
2
Identify the answer
The answer lists specific deeds disbelievers can perform that do not require intention: maintaining family ties, helping others, freeing slaves.
These deeds are placed in the scale against their sins that are not disbelief.
3
Examine the nuance about disbelief
Disbelief itself is not weighed because its punishment is eternal. Al-Qurtubi clarifies that even if these deeds are weighed, disbelief tips the scale.
This shows the balance still favors punishment due to disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : وزنُ أعمال المؤمنين وجهه ظاهرُ ؛ إذ له من الحسنات ما يقابل السيئات، وأما الكفار فليس لهم حسنات حتى تقابل بها سيئاتهم.. أجيب بأنه يكون منهم صلة الرحم ومواساة الناس وعتق المماليك ونحوها من الأعمال التي لا تتوقف صحتها على نيَّة، فتجعل هذه الأمور - إن صدرت منهم - في مقابلة سيئاتهم غير الكفر ، أما هو فلا فائدة في وزنه؛ لأن عذابه دائم، وفي كلام القرطبي ما يُصرح بوزنه، حيث قال : فتجمع له هذه الأمور وتوضع في ميزانه ؛ يعني الكافر، فيرجح الكفر بها ، انتهى .
If it is said: The weighing of the deeds of believers is apparent in its aspect, because they have good deeds to counterbalance bad deeds. As for the disbelievers, they have no good deeds to counterbalance their bad deeds. It is answered: They perform acts like maintaining family ties, helping people, freeing slaves, and similar deeds that do not depend on intention for their validity. These matters – if they occur from them – are placed in counterbalance of their sins other than disbelief. As for disbelief itself, there is no benefit in weighing it because its punishment is eternal. In the words of al-Qurtubi, he explicitly states its weighing, where he said: These matters are gathered for him and placed in his scale – meaning the disbeliever – and then disbelief outweighs them. End.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the weighing of deeds of disbelievers. The objector argues that weighing deeds is meaningful only for believers because they have both good and bad deeds to compare, while disbelievers have no good deeds. The answer explains that disbelievers do perform some virtuous acts such as maintaining family ties, showing compassion to people, and freeing slaves – deeds that are valid regardless of intention. These are placed in the scale against their sins that are not directly disbelief. As for disbelief itself, weighing it is pointless because its punishment is eternal. The commentator then quotes al-Qurtubi, who affirms that these good deeds are gathered and placed in the disbeliever’s scale, and despite them, his disbelief outweighs them. The answer thus clarifies that weighing applies even to disbelievers for certain deeds, but disbelief itself is not weighed due to its permanent punishment.

صلة الرحم
حسنات
سيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disbelievers have no good deeds at all, so their scale is empty.
Correction: Disbelievers do have certain virtuous deeds (like family ties, charity) that are weighed against their non-kufr sins.

Checkpoint

B) Because they perform deeds like maintaining family ties, which do not require intention.
C) Because their disbelief is also weighed to increase punishment.
D) Because they have no good deeds, so only bad deeds are weighed.

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers' virtuous deeds are weighed in the scale against sins other than disbelief; disbelief itself is not weighed because its punishment is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You perform many good deeds daily—how many of them would stand even if your intention were imperfect? The passage says that deeds like helping others are valid without intention. Does this comfort you or challenge you? What does it reveal about Allah's mercy?
?

?
The passage says disbelief is not weighed because its punishment is eternal. This means no amount of good deeds can tip the scale against kufr. Are you absolutely certain that your heart is free from any form of disbelief that you tolerate? What one step will you take today to strengthen your iman?

Weighing the Books of Deeds: The Majority Opinion
The majority opinion that what is weighed are the books recording deeds, with the hadith of the card

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that the books recording deeds are weighed on the Day of Judgment, and the evidence from the hadith of the card.

Core Idea
The scales weigh the records of deeds, not the deeds themselves.

The author presents the first of two opinions on what is placed in the scale on the Day of Judgment. The majority of exegetes hold that it is the books containing the deeds of the servants. This opinion is supported by a powerful hadith in which a single card bearing the shahada outweighs countless registers of sins. As we study this passage, we will explore both the opinion and the hadith, along with an important qualification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two opinions
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author introduces two opinions: that the books are weighed or the deeds themselves. The focus here is on the first opinion.
The passage is from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid.
2
Analyze the hadith of the card
Read the hadith carefully. Understand the story: a man with ninety-nine huge registers of sins is saved by a small card with the shahada. Reflect on how this illustrates the majority opinion.
The hadith is from Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As.
3
Reflect on the qualification
The author notes that this hadith is not for every servant, but only for one whom God intends good. Consider why this qualification is important and how it guards against misunderstanding.
This shows that the hadith is not a blanket promise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَتُوْزَنُ الكُتُبُ أَوِ الأَعْيانُ) أشار بذلك إلى اختلاف العلماء في الموزون، فذهب جمهور المفسرين إلى أن الموزون الكتب التي اشتملت على أعمال العباد، بناء على أن الحسنات مميزة بكتاب والسيئات بآخر ويشهد له حديث البطاقة، بكسر الموحدة وهي ورقة صغيرة وحديثها : ما رُوي عن عبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : إن الله يستخلص رجلاً من أمتي على رؤوس الخلائق يوم القيامة، فيُنْشَرُ عليه تسعة وتسعون سِجَلا ، كل سجل منها مدُّ البصر، ثم يقول : أتنكرُ من هذا شيئاً؟ أظلمك كتبتي الحافظون؟ فيقول: لا يا رَبِّ، فيقولُ: ألك عُذر؟ فيقول : لا يا رَبِّ، فيقول : ألك حسنةٌ؟ فيقول : لا يا رَبِّ، فيقول : بلى إن لك عندنا لحسنة، وإنه لا ظُلمَ عليك، فتخرج له بطاقة كالأنملة فيها (أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأشهد أن محمداً رسول الله، فيقول: يا رَبِّ ؛ ما هذه البطاقة مع هذه السجلات؟ فيُقال: إنك لا تُظلم، فتوضع السجلات في كِفَّةٍ والبطاقة في كِفَّةٍ، فطاشت السجلات وثقلت البطاقة، ولا ينقل مع اسم الله شيء، انتهى . وهذا ليس لكلِّ عبد، بل لعبد أراد الله به خيراً. وليس في الحديث : أن البطاقة ورقة صغيرة قدر الأنملة .
His saying: (Then the books or the deeds themselves are weighed) – he indicated by that the difference of scholars concerning what is weighed. The majority of exegetes hold that what is weighed are the books that contain the deeds of the servants, based on the fact that good deeds are distinguished in one book and bad deeds in another. This is attested by the hadith of the card (bitaqa), with a kasra on the first letter, which is a small paper. Its hadith: It is narrated from Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, that he said: Indeed, God will isolate a man from my community before the entire creation on the Day of Resurrection, and ninety-nine registers will be spread out for him, each register as far as the eye can see. Then He will say: Do you deny any of this? Have my recording scribes wronged you? He will say: No, my Lord. He will say: Do you have an excuse? He will say: No, my Lord. He will say: Do you have a good deed? He will say: No, my Lord. He will say: Rather, you have a good deed with Us, and there is no injustice upon you. Then a card like a fingertip will be brought out for him containing: I bear witness that there is no god but God and I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of God. He will say: My Lord, what is this card alongside these registers? It will be said: You will not be wronged. Then the registers will be placed on one pan and the card on another pan, and the registers will fly up while the card becomes heavy, and nothing is weighed with the name of God. End. And this is not for every servant, but for a servant for whom God intends good. And in the hadith, it does not say that the card is a small paper of the size of a fingertip.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two opinions regarding what is weighed on the Day of Judgment. The author quotes the original line 'Then the books or the deeds themselves are weighed' and explains that scholars differ on the subject. The majority of exegetes hold that what is weighed are the books (records) that contain the deeds of the servants, on the basis that good deeds are recorded in one book and bad deeds in another. As evidence, he cites the well-known 'hadith of the card' (hadith al-bitaqa) from Abdullah ibn Amr. In this hadith, a man has ninety-nine registers of sins, but a single card containing the testimony of faith outweighs them all. The author then notes that the hadith specifically applies only to a servant whom God intends good, and he also clarifies that the hadith does not say the card is a small paper the size of a fingertip, though that is often understood. This opinion emphasizes that the actual records of deeds are placed in the scale, not the deeds themselves.

الكتب
الأعمال
البطاقة
الشهادة
الميزان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of the card means that any Muslim who says the shahada will be saved regardless of sins.
Correction: The text explicitly states that this is not for every servant, but only for one whom God intends good. It is not a blanket promise.

Checkpoint
According to the majority opinion presented, what is weighed in the scale?
A) The deeds themselves in physical form.
B) The books containing the deeds.
C) The person himself.
D) Both the books and the deeds.

Key Takeaway
The majority opinion holds that the books of deeds are weighed, and the hadith of the card demonstrates that a sincere shahada can outweigh countless sins for those whom God intends good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were that man standing with ninety-nine registers of sins, what would you be thinking when God asks if you have any excuse? Are you living today as if you might have an answer?
?
The hadith says this man had a card with the shahada that outweighed everything. Is your shahada just a phrase you say, or is it a reality that fills your scales? What would need to change for it to be that heavy?
?
This special treatment is only for a servant 'whom God intends good'. Do you want God to intend good for you? Look at your life right now: is there evidence that you want good from Him—or are you relying on a mere hope without action? What one step will you take today to show Him you are serious?

The Weighing of Deeds as Forms: Defending a Minority View
Minority opinion on weighing deeds as forms, with defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the minority opinion that deeds themselves are weighed as luminous or dark forms, the rational objection to this view, and how scholars defended its possibility.

Core Idea
Deeds become visible forms in the scale without violating rational possibility.

The majority view holds that only records of deeds are weighed, but some scholars argued that the deeds themselves—good and bad—take on physical forms to be placed on the scale. This raises an important question: does this involve 'changing realities,' which is rationally impossible? The passage below explains the view, defends it, and adds further nuances about who may be weighed and why.

Guided Path
1
Understand the minority view
Read the opening sentence: 'Some others went to the view that what is weighed are the deeds themselves (a'yan al-a'mal).' Notice that deeds are represented as beautiful or ugly forms.
This is an alternative to weighing scrolls.
2
Follow the weighing process
Righteous deeds become luminous forms placed on the right pan; evil deeds become dark forms on the left pan. For believers, good deeds become heavy by grace; for disbelievers, the opposite occurs by justice.
Contrast between grace (fadl) and justice ('adl).
3
Grasp the objection and its refutation
The objection is that this would involve 'changing realities' (qalb al-haqa'iq), which is impossible. The answer specifies that impossibility applies only to rational categories (e.g., obligatory cannot become permissible); a meaning (ma'na) turning into a substance (jirm) is not impossible.
The objection is narrow; physical transformation is allowed.
4
Consider additional views
Some say God creates bodies equal in number to the deeds; others say the person himself may be weighed, citing Ibn Mas'ud's hadith about his leg being heavier than Mount Uhud.
These views are additional, not contradictory.
5
Reflect on the purpose of weighing
The passage ends with the benefits: a sign for happiness/misery, informing servants of their account, and establishing proof against them.
Weighing serves both mercy and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الموزون أعيان الأعمال، فتصوّرُ الأعمال الصالحة بصورة حسنة نورانيَّة، ثم تُطرحُ في كِفَّة النور؛ وهي اليمنى المعدة للحسنات، فتثقل بفضل الله سبحانه وتعالى، وتصوّر الأعمال السيئة بصورة قبيحة ظلمانيَّة، ثم تُطرح في كِفَّة الظلمة ؛ الشمال المعدة للسيئات فتَخِتُ، وهذا في المؤمن. وأما الكافر فتَخِفُ حسناته، وتثقل سيئاته بعدل الله سبحانه وتعالى. ولا يرد أن في ذلك قلب الحقائق، وهو ممتنع ؛ لأن امتناع قلب الحقائق مختص بأقسام الحكم العقلي، فلا ينقلب الواجب جائزاً مثلاً، وأما انقلاب المعنى جرماً فلا يمتنع . وقيل : يخلق الله أجساماً على عدد تلك الأعمال من غير قلب لها . قيل : وقد يوزنُ الشخص نفسه؛ لحديث ابن مسعود رجلُه في الميزان أثقل من جبل أحده. وفائدة الوزن : جعله علامةً لأهل السعادة والشقاوة، وتعريف العباد ما لهم وما عليهم من الخير والشر، وإقامة الحجة عليهم.
Some others went to the view that what is weighed are the deeds themselves (a'yan al-a'mal). So righteous deeds are represented in a beautiful luminous form, then placed on the pan of light – which is the right pan prepared for good deeds – and they become heavy by the grace of God Almighty. And evil deeds are represented in an ugly dark form, then placed on the pan of darkness – the left pan prepared for bad deeds – and they become light (or heavy? text says 'fatakhittu' which likely means light). This is for the believer. As for the disbeliever, his good deeds become light and his bad deeds become heavy by the justice of God Almighty. And it is not objected that this involves the changing of realities (qalb al-haqa'iq), which is impossible, because the impossibility of changing realities is specific to the categories of rational judgment; thus the obligatory cannot become permissible, for example. As for a meaning turning into a substance, that is not impossible. And it is said: God creates bodies equal in number to those deeds without changing them. And it is said: The person himself may be weighed, based on the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud that his leg is heavier in the scale than Mount Uhud. And the benefit of weighing is to make it a sign for the people of happiness and misery, and to inform the servants of what they have and what is against them of good and evil, and to establish the proof against them.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion on what is weighed: the deeds themselves (a'yan al-a'mal) rather than records. According to this view, each good deed is represented in a beautiful luminous form and placed on the right pan (the pan of light), where it becomes heavy by God's grace. Each evil deed is represented in an ugly dark form and placed on the left pan (the pan of darkness), where it becomes light for the believer. For the disbeliever, the opposite occurs: his good deeds become light and his bad deeds become heavy by God's justice. The author then addresses an objection: that this would involve 'changing realities' (qalb al-haqa'iq), which is impossible. He answers that the impossibility of changing realities applies only to the categories of rational judgment (e.g., the obligatory cannot become permissible), but turning a meaning (ma'na) into a substance (jirm) is not impossible. He then mentions two additional views: some say that God creates bodies equal in number to the deeds without changing them; others say that the person himself may be weighed, citing the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud. Finally, he states the purpose of the weighing: to serve as a sign of happiness or misery, to inform servants of their account, and to establish the proof against them. The passage thus defends the plausibility of weighing deeds as forms against rational objections and provides a comprehensive view of the eschatological scale.

Key Terms
أعيان الأعمال
قلب الحقائق
الحكم العقلي
الميزان
الشقاوة
السعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The changing of realities is always impossible, so deeds cannot become forms.
Correction: The impossibility of 'changing realities' applies only to the categories of rational judgment (e.g., the obligatory cannot become permissible). Transforming a meaning into a substance is not forbidden, so the representation of deeds as forms is rationally possible.

Checkpoint
According to the minority opinion, how are good deeds represented for weighing?
A) As a dark form placed on the left pan.
B) As a beautiful luminous form placed on the right pan.
C) As a written book placed in the scale.
D) As the person himself.

Key Takeaway
The weighing of deeds as luminous or dark forms is defended as rational because the impossibility of changing realities applies only to rational categories, not to physical representation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your good deeds were transformed into luminous forms right now, would they be heavy enough to tip the scale? What is one deed you can do today that would add weight to that pan?
?
The passage says evil deeds become light for the believer by God's grace. Do you rely more on God's grace or on your own efforts to lighten your burden of sins? How can you actively trust His grace today?
?
Imagine standing before the scale, and your deeds appear as visible forms. Which form would embarrass you most? What change will you make starting now so that form is replaced by something beautiful?

The Card of Shahadah Outweighs All Sins
Sanusi relates the hadith of the card to illustrate the weight of shahadah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the hadith of the card and that the mercy of Allah can outweigh countless sins through sincere faith.

Core Idea
A single sincere shahadah can outweigh an ocean of sins by Allah's mercy.

This lesson presents the famous prophetic tradition about a man whose sins were like huge registers, but a small card with the shahadah outweighed them all. It teaches that Allah's mercy is not limited by arithmetic, but is granted to whom He wills. We will examine the hadith and its qualification that this is not for everyone.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dramatic contrast between the small card and the large registers.
2
Understand the imagery of the balance
The hadith uses the image of two pans of a scale. The card and registers are placed in separate pans. Observe which side becomes heavy and which becomes light.
The card outweighs the registers, showing the power of sincere faith.
3
Note the qualification
The author adds that this is not for every servant, but only for one whom Allah intends good. This prevents misunderstanding that the hadith is a mechanical guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات
وإنه لا ظُلمَ عليك، فتخرج له بطاقة كالأنملة فيها (أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأشهد أن محمداً رسول الله، فيقول: يا رَبِّ ؛ ما هذه البطاقة مع هذه السجلات؟ فيُقال: إنك لا تُظلم، فتوضع السجلات في كِفَّةٍ والبطاقة في كِفَّةٍ، فطاشت السجلات وثقلت البطاقة، ولا ينقل مع اسم الله شيء، انتهى . وهذا ليس لكلِّ عبد، بل لعبد أراد الله به خيراً وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الموزون أعيان الأعمال، فتصوّرُ الأعمال الصالحة بصورة حسنة نورانيَّة، ثم تُطرحُ في كِفَّة النور؛ وهي اليمنى المعدة للحسنات، فتثقل بفضل الله سبحانه وتعالى، وتصوّر الأعمال السيئة بصورة قبيحة ظلمانيَّة، ثم تُطرح في كِفَّة الظلمة ; الشمال المعدة للسيئات فتَخ[؟]، وهذا في المؤمن. وأما الكافر فتَخِفُ حسناته، وتثقل سيئاته بعدل الله سبحانه وتعالى.
ولا يرد أن في ذلك قلب الحقائق، وهو ممتنع ; لأن امتناع قلب الحقائق مختص بأقسام الحكم العقلي، فلا ينقلب الواجب جائزاً مثلاً، وأما انقلاب المعنى جرماً فلا يمتنع .
وقيل : يخلق الله أجساماً على عدد تلك الأعمال من غير قلب لها . قيل : وقد يوزنُ الشخص نفسه؛ لحديث ابن مسعود رجلُه في الميزان أثقل من جبل أحده.
وفائدة الوزن : جعله علامةً لأهل السعادة والشقاوة، وتعريف العباد ما لهم وما عليهم من الخير والشر، وإقامة الحجة عليهم.
The auditory matters. And that there is no injustice upon you, so there is brought out for him a card like a fingertip containing 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah, and I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.' He says: 'O Lord, what is this card with these registers?' It is said: 'You are not wronged.' So the registers are placed in a pan and the card in a pan, so the registers become light and the card becomes heavy, and nothing outweighs the name of Allah. End. And this is not for every servant, but for a servant whom Allah intends good. And some went to that what is weighed is the essences of deeds: the righteous deeds are formed into a beautiful luminous form, then thrown into the pan of light – which is the right pan prepared for good deeds – so they become heavy by the bounty of Allah Almighty, and the evil deeds are formed into an ugly dark form, then thrown into the pan of darkness – the left pan prepared for evil deeds – so they become light. And this is for the believer. As for the disbeliever, his good deeds become light and his evil deeds become heavy by the justice of Allah Almighty. And it is not objected that this involves changing realities, which is impossible; because the impossibility of changing realities is specific to the divisions of rational judgment: the obligatory does not become permissible, for example. As for the transformation of a meaning into a substance, it is not impossible. And it is said: Allah creates bodies equal to the number of those deeds without changing them. It is said: And the person himself may be weighed, due to the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud: 'His foot in the balance is heavier than Mount Uhud.' The benefit of weighing is that it is made a sign for the people of happiness and misery, and to inform the servants what they have and against them of good and evil, and to establish the proof against them.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage discusses the weighing of deeds on the Day of Judgment, a matter from the samiyyat (audible matters of faith). It begins by relating the famous hadith of the card (al-bitaqah) from the Prophet, where a man with many sins but sincere shahadah on a small card outweighs them. The author explains that this favor is not for everyone but for whom Allah wills. Some scholars hold that the actual essences of deeds are weighed: good deeds are transformed into beautiful luminous forms and placed in the right pan of the balance, becoming heavy by Allah's grace; evil deeds become dark ugly forms and are placed in the left pan, becoming light for the believer. For the disbeliever, his good deeds become light and evil deeds heavy, by Allah's justice. An objection is raised that this involves changing realities (qalb al-haqa'iq), which is rationally impossible. The author refutes this by clarifying that the impossibility of changing realities applies only to rational judgments (e.g., the obligatory cannot become permissible). However, transforming an abstract meaning into a physical substance is not impossible for Allah. Another opinion is that Allah creates separate bodies corresponding to each deed without altering the deeds themselves. Yet another view holds that the person himself may be weighed, as in the hadith about Ibn Mas'ud's foot. Finally, the purpose of the weighing is stated: to distinguish the people of happiness from the people of misery, to inform servants of their good and evil, and to establish the proof against them.

السجلات
كفة
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the hadith implies anyone who says shahadah will automatically be saved regardless of their sins.
Correction: The author explicitly says 'this is not for every servant, but for a servant whom Allah intends good' – showing it is a specific mercy, not a guarantee.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith, what is written on the card that outweighs the registers of sins?
A) The entire Quran
B) The shahadah
C) The names of all prophets
D) A list of good deeds

Key Takeaway
The hadith of the card shows that sincere faith, by Allah's mercy, can outweigh a lifetime of sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your shahadah is heavier than your sins, or do you secretly rely on your own deeds? How does this lesson challenge your daily trust in Allah's mercy versus your fear of His justice? Write one specific situation today where you will consciously rely on your shahadah instead of your own righteousness.
?
The hadith says this card is for a servant whom Allah intends good. What does that reveal about your own sincerity? Are you content to simply say the words, or do you deeply desire that Allah intends good for you? What one habit can you start to sincerely show that you are a servant Allah intends good for?
?
If your deeds were placed on the scale right now, would your shahadah outweigh them? This lesson says 'nothing outweighs the name of Allah.' How does that make you feel? Are you comforted or uneasy? Commit to one action today to make your shahadah more alive in your heart, not just on your tongue.

Deeds Weighed as Luminous and Dark Forms
Sanusi presents the view of some scholars on how deeds are weighed in the form of transformed essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view that deeds are transformed into physical forms and weighed in the balance, and the difference between believers and disbelievers in this process.

Core Idea
Allah transforms good deeds into light and evil deeds into darkness for weighing.

Some scholars explain that the weighing of deeds involves transforming each deed into a luminous or dark form corresponding to its nature. Good deeds become beautiful light and are placed in the right pan of the balance, while evil deeds become ugly darkness in the left pan. This lesson explores that view and notes the difference for believers and disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Know the view that essences of deeds are weighed
Read the passage identifying the opinion: 'what is weighed is the essences of deeds.' Understand that this means the actual deeds themselves are transformed and placed on the scale.
2
Visualize the transformation into forms
The passage describes good deeds becoming beautiful luminous forms and evil deeds becoming ugly dark forms. Notice which pan each is thrown into and why.
The right pan is for good deeds; the left for evil deeds.
3
Compare believer vs disbeliever outcome
For the believer, good deeds become heavy by Allah's bounty; for the disbeliever, his good deeds become light and evil deeds heavy by Allah's justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So the registers are placed in a pan and the card in a pan, so the registers become light and the card becomes heavy, and nothing outweighs the name of Allah. End. And this is not for every servant, but for a servant whom Allah intends good. And some went to that what is weighed is the essences of deeds: the righteous deeds are formed into a beautiful luminous form, then thrown into the pan of light – which is the right pan prepared for good deeds – so they become heavy by the bounty of Allah Almighty, and the evil deeds are formed into an ugly dark form, then thrown into the pan of darkness – the left pan prepared for evil deeds – so they become light. And this is for the believer. As for the disbeliever, his good deeds become light and his evil deeds become heavy by the justice of Allah Almighty. And it is not objected that this involves changing realities, which is impossible; because the impossibility of changing realities is specific to the divisions of rational judgment: the obligatory does not become permissible, for example. As for the transformation of a meaning into a substance, it is not impossible. And it is said: Allah creates bodies equal to the number of those deeds without changing them. It is said: And the person himself may be weighed, due to the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud: 'His foot in the balance is heavier than Mount Uhud.' The benefit of weighing is that it is made a sign for the people of happiness and misery, and to inform the servants what they have and against them of good and evil, and to establish the proof against them.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage discusses the weighing of deeds on the Day of Judgment, a matter from the samiyyat (audible matters of faith). It begins by relating the famous hadith of the card (al-bitaqah) from the Prophet, where a man with many sins but sincere shahadah on a small card outweighs them. The author explains that this favor is not for everyone but for whom Allah wills. Some scholars hold that the actual essences of deeds are weighed: good deeds are transformed into beautiful luminous forms and placed in the right pan of the balance, becoming heavy by Allah's grace; evil deeds become dark ugly forms and are placed in the left pan, becoming light for the believer. For the disbeliever, his good deeds become light and evil deeds heavy, by Allah's justice. An objection is raised that this involves changing realities (qalb al-haqa'iq), which is rationally impossible. The author refutes this by clarifying that the impossibility of changing realities applies only to rational judgments (e.g., the obligatory cannot become permissible). However, transforming an abstract meaning into a physical substance is not impossible for Allah. Another opinion is that Allah creates separate bodies corresponding to each deed without altering the deeds themselves. Yet another view holds that the person himself may be weighed, as in the hadith about Ibn Mas'ud's foot. Finally, the purpose of the weighing is stated: to distinguish the people of happiness from the people of misery, to inform servants of their good and evil, and to establish the proof against them.

Key Terms
أعيان الأعمال
تصوّر
نوراني
ظلماني
كفة النور
كفة الظلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Where are the evil deeds of a believer placed in the balance?
A) Right pan
B) Left pan
C) Both pans
D) Not placed

Key Takeaway
The weighing of deeds involves their transformation into forms that reflect their moral quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you imagine it being transformed into a radiant form that will one day be weighed heavy in your favor? If not, what does that say about how seriously you take the reality of the Hereafter?
?
Consider a sin you often commit. If you saw it now as an ugly, dark form that will be placed on the scale, would you still commit it? What is holding you back from truly internalizing this image?
?
For the disbeliever, good deeds become light despite being good. What does this teach you about the absolute necessity of faith for the weight of your deeds? Examine your heart: are you relying on your deeds or on Allah's mercy through faith?

Answering the Objection and the Wisdom Behind Weighing
Sanusi addresses and refutes the objection to transformation of deeds, then states the benefit of the balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that weighing transformed deeds involves changing realities, how Sanusi refutes it, and the stated purpose of the weighing.

Core Idea
Changing a meaning into a substance is not impossible, and weighing has a clear purpose.

An objection could arise: transforming an abstract deed into a physical form seems like changing the reality of the deed, which is impossible. Sanusi clarifies that this change is not of the types of rational judgments (e.g., making the obligatory permissible), but a conversion of a meaning into a substance, which Allah can do. He also mentions other opinions and concludes with the wisdom behind the weighing: to distinguish the saved and the damned, inform, and establish proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection about qalb al-haqa'iq
Read the objection: 'this involves changing realities, which is impossible.' Consider why someone might think this is a problem.
2
See the distinction Sanusi makes
Sanusi limits the impossibility to rational judgments (e.g., an obligatory act cannot become permissible). Transforming a meaning into a substance is not in that category.
This is a key theological distinction.
3
Note alternative opinions
Sanusi mentions that some say Allah creates separate bodies for deeds without transformation, and others say the person himself may be weighed.
These show scholarly diversity.
4
Learn the purpose of weighing
The passage ends with three benefits: as a sign for happiness/misery, to inform servants of their deeds, and to establish proof against them.

السمعيات
وإنه لا ظُلمَ عليك، فتخرج له بطاقة كالأنملة فيها (أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأشهد أن محمداً رسول الله، فيقول: يا رَبِّ ؛ ما هذه البطاقة مع هذه السجلات؟ فيُقال: إنك لا تُظلم، فتوضع السجلات في كِفَّةٍ والبطاقة في كِفَّةٍ، فطاشت السجلات وثقلت البطاقة، ولا ينقل مع اسم الله شيء، انتهى . وهذا ليس لكلِّ عبد، بل لعبد أراد الله به خيراً وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الموزون أعيان الأعمال، فتصوّرُ الأعمال الصالحة بصورة حسنة نورانيَّة، ثم تُطرحُ في كِفَّة النور؛ وهي اليمنى المعدة للحسنات، فتثقل بفضل الله سبحانه وتعالى، وتصوّر الأعمال السيئة بصورة قبيحة ظلمانيَّة، ثم تُطرح في كِفَّة الظلمة ; الشمال المعدة للسيئات فتَخ[؟]، وهذا في المؤمن. وأما الكافر فتَخِفُ حسناته، وتثقل سيئاته بعدل الله سبحانه وتعالى.

And that there is no injustice upon you, so there is brought out for him a card like a fingertip containing 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah, and I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.' ?

?

What one hidden flaw are you unwilling to confront, and what will you do to change it starting today?

The Sirat: Definition and Universal Crossing
Definition of the Sirat and the passing of all people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Sirat is a bridge over Hell that all people must cross, and its linguistic and legal meanings.

Core Idea
The Sirat is an unavoidable bridge over Hell, and even the prophets pray for safety.

This lesson introduces the Sirat, one of the auditory matters of the afterlife. The author explains the word's derivation, its definition as a bridge over Hell, and the fact that all people—including disbelievers—will come to it. He also notes that only the prophets speak, imploring Allah for safety.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's own words come through.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
Note that the word 'sirat' comes from 'sarata' meaning to swallow, because the path swallows those who pass.
3
Recognize the legal definition
The legal definition: a bridge stretched over Hell that all people must cross. This is an obligation based on revelation.
4
Notice the silent prophets
All are silent except the prophets, who say: 'O Allah, grant safety, grant safety.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصراط والمُرورُ عليه
كَذَا الصِّراط فالعِبادُ مُخْتَلِف مُرُورُهُمْ فَسَالِمٌ ومُنْتَلِفٌ
قوله : (كَذَا الصِّراط) (كذا) خبر مقدَّم، و(الصراط): مبتدأ مؤخر ; أي: الصراط مثل المذكور من أخذ العباد الصحف والوزن والميزان في الوجوب السمعي؛ وهو بالصاد أو بالسين أو بالزاي المحضة أو بالإشمام، وقُرئ في السبع بما عدا الزاي المحضة ومعناه لغةً : الطريق الواضح، مأخوذ من صرطه يصرطه إذا ابتلعه؛ لأنه يبتلع المارة .
وشرعاً : جِسْرٌ ممدود على متن جهنم يَرِدُهُ الأوّلون والآخرون حتى الكفار، خلافاً للحليمي حيث ذهب إلى أنهم لا يمرون عليه، ولعله أراد الطائفة التي تُرمى في جهنم من الموقف بلا صراط وشمل ما ذكر النبيين والصديقين ومَنْ يدخل الجنة بغير حساب، وكلهم ساكتون إلا الأنبياء، فيقولون: اللهم سَلّمْ سَلّمْ ; كما في «الصحيح».
وفي بعض الروايات: أنه أدقَّ من الشعرة وأحد من السيف، وهو المشهور.
ونازعَ في ذلك العز بن عبد السلام والشيخ القرافي وغيرهما كالبدر الزركشي، قالوا: وعلى فرض صحة ذلك فهو محمول على غير ظاهره بأن يؤول بأنه كنايةٌ عن شدة المشقة، وحينئذٍ فلا ينافي ما ورد من الأحاديث الدالة على قيام الملائكة على جنبيه، وكون الكلاليب فيه.
زاد القَرَافِيُّ : والصحيح أنه عريض، وفيه طريقان يمنى ويسرى، فأهل السعادة ذات اليمين وأهل الشقاوة يسلك بهم ذات الشمال، وفيه طاقات كل طاقة تنفذ إلى طبقة من طبقات جهنم، وقال بعضهم: إنه يَدِقُ ويتسعُ بحسب ضيق النور وانتشاره فعرض صراط كل أحد بقدر انتشار نوره، فإن نور كل إنسان لا يتعداه إلى غيره، فلا يمشي أحد في نور أحدٍ، ومن هنا كان دقيقاً في حق قوم، وعريضاً في حق آخرین وطوله ثلاثة آلاف سنة : ألفُ صعود، وألف هبوط، وألف استواء . وفي كلام الشيخ الأكبر ما يفيد عدم التعويل على ظاهر هذه الآلاف، أن ماله الامتداد للعلق، حتى يصل إلى الجنة؛ فإنها عالية جدا.
The Bridge and the Passing Over It
Thus the Bridge – the servants' passing is varied: some safe and some differing.
His saying: 'Thus the Bridge' – 'Thus' is a forward predicate, and 'the Bridge' is a delayed subject; i.e., the Bridge is like what was mentioned of the taking of records and the weighing and the balance in terms of auditory obligation. It is with (sad) or (sin) or pure (zay) or with indication, and it was recited in the Seven (readings) with other than pure (zay). Its linguistic meaning is the clear path, derived from 'saratahu' meaning he swallowed it, because it swallows those who pass.
And legally: a bridge stretched over the surface of Hell, which the first and last come to, even the disbelievers, contrary to al-Halimi who held that they do not pass over it. Perhaps he meant the group that is thrown into Hell from the standing-place without a bridge. And it includes what was mentioned: the prophets, the truthful, and those who enter Paradise without account. All of them are silent except the prophets, who say: 'O Allah, grant safety, grant safety' – as in the Sahih.
And in some narrations: it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, and this is the well-known view.
But al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Shaykh al-Qarafi, and others like al-Badr al-Zarkashi disputed that. They said: Assuming its authenticity, it is carried on other than its apparent meaning, being interpreted as a metaphor for severe difficulty. In that case, it does not contradict the hadiths that indicate the angels standing on its sides and the hooks in it.
Al-Qarafi added: The correct view is that it is wide, and in it are two roads: right and left. The people of happiness take the right, and the people of misery are led along the left. In it are openings, each opening leading to a layer of Hell. And some said: It becomes narrow and wide according to the narrowness and expansion of light, so the width of each person's bridge is according to the spread of his light. Indeed, the light of each person does not extend to another, so no one walks in another's light. Hence it is narrow for a group and wide for another. Its length is three thousand years: a thousand ascending, a thousand descending, and a thousand level. In the words of al-Shaykh al-Akbar (Ibn Arabi) is what indicates not relying on the apparent meaning of these thousands, for it has extension until reaching Paradise, for it is very high.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage discusses the Bridge (al-Sirat) over Hell, another essential matter of samiyyat. It begins by explaining the word 'sirat', which is pronounced with different letters. Linguistically it means the clear path, derived from the verb 'sarata' meaning to swallow, because it swallows those who pass. Legally, it is a bridge stretched over Hell, which all people come to, including disbelievers – contrary to al-Halimi's view that disbelievers do not pass over it; perhaps he meant a specific group that is cast into Hell directly from the standing place. The passage includes the prophets, truthful ones, and those entering Paradise without account. All are silent except the prophets, who supplicate for safety. The well-known description is that it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword. However, scholars like al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, and al-Zarkashi disputed this literal interpretation, arguing that if such hadiths are authentic, they must be interpreted metaphorically to indicate extreme difficulty, so as not to contradict other hadiths about angels standing at its sides and hooks. Al-Qarafi added that the correct view is that the Sirat is wide, with two roads: right for the blessed and left for the damned, and it has openings leading to layers of Hell. Some say its narrowness and width vary according to the light of each person: each person's bridge width equals the spread of his own light, which is exclusive to him. Thus it is narrow for some and wide for others. Its length is described as 3000 years (up, down, level), but the Shaykh al-Akbar (Ibn Arabi) advises not to take these numbers literally, as the bridge extends to Paradise, which is very high.

Key Terms
الصراط
الجسر
المرور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sirat is only for believers to cross.
Correction: The passage states that even disbelievers come to it, though some scholars have dissented.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of al-Sirat?
A) A narrow path of light
B) A bridge stretched over Hell
C) A scale for weighing deeds
D) A record of deeds

Key Takeaway
The Sirat is an obligated belief that all people will cross, and its name comes from 'swallowing' those who pass.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophets, the best of creation, are silent when faced with the Sirat except to beg 'O Allah, grant safety'. You are not a prophet. How should this affect your confidence about crossing? What would you say to Allah at that moment? Start practicing that now.
?
If even the sinless prophets fear the crossing, what does your own negligence about sin say about your preparation? Identify one sin you keep returning to that will weigh you down on that narrow bridge. Will you not repent from it today?
?
The Sirat swallows those who pass. Your life is the path to that bridge. Are you walking on a path that leads to a safe crossing, or are you walking on a path that will make you fall? Write down one concrete change you must make before you stand there.

The Sirat: Thinness, Width, and Scholarly Interpretations
Describes the thinness, sharpness, scholarly dispute, width based on light, and length of the Sirat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the well-known description of the Sirat as thinner than a hair and sharper than a sword, the scholarly debate about literalism, and the alternative view that its width depends on each person's light.

Core Idea
The Sirat's thinness is a metaphor for difficulty, and its width varies according to the believer's light.

This lesson delves into the physical descriptions of the Sirat found in narrations. It presents the widely held view that it is extremely thin and sharp, but also introduces the critique by renowned scholars who interpret these descriptions metaphorically. It then presents an alternative understanding that the Sirat's width is relative to each person's light and concludes with a note on its length.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the thinness debate
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the contrast between the well-known view and the scholarly criticism.
2
Understand the metaphoric interpretation
Scholars like al-'Izz and al-Qarafi argue that thinness is a metaphor for severe hardship, not literal physicality.
3
Examine al-Qarafi's addition
Al-Qarafi asserts the Sirat is wide with two roads and openings to Hell. This is a distinct view.
4
Grasp the light-based width
Each person's bridge width equals the spread of their own light. It is narrow for some, wide for others.
5
Consider the length and Ibn Arabi's caution
The length is given as 3000 years, but Shaykh al-Akbar advises not to take it literally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصراط والمُرورُ عليه
كَذَا الصِّراط فالعِبادُ مُخْتَلِف مُرُورُهُمْ فَسَالِمٌ ومُنْتَلِفٌ
قوله : (كَذَا الصِّراط) (كذا) خبر مقدَّم، و(الصراط): مبتدأ مؤخر ; أي: الصراط مثل المذكور من أخذ العباد الصحف والوزن والميزان في الوجوب السمعي؛ وهو بالصاد أو بالسين أو بالزاي المحضة أو بالإشمام، وقُرئ في السبع بما عدا الزاي المحضة ومعناه لغةً : الطريق الواضح، مأخوذ من صرطه يصرطه إذا ابتلعه؛ لأنه يبتلع المارة .

وفي بعض الروايات: أنه أدقَّ من الشعرة وأحد من السيف، وهو المشهور.
ونازعَ في ذلك العز بن عبد السلام والشيخ القرافي وغيرهما كالبدر الزركشي، قالوا: وعلى فرض صحة ذلك فهو محمول على غير ظاهره بأن يؤول بأنه كنايةٌ عن شدة المشقة، وحينئذٍ فلا ينافي ما ورد من الأحاديث الدالة على قيام الملائكة على جنبيه، وكون الكلاليب فيه.
زاد القَرَافِيُّ : والصحيح أنه عريض، وفيه طريقان يمنى ويسرى، فأهل السعادة ذات اليمين وأهل الشقاوة يسلك بهم ذات الشمال، وفيه طاقات كل طاقة تنفذ إلى طبقة من طبقات جهنم، وقال بعضهم: إنه يَدِقُ ويتسعُ بحسب ضيق النور وانتشاره فعرض صراط كل أحد بقدر انتشار نوره، فإن نور كل إنسان لا يتعداه إلى غيره، فلا يمشي أحد في نور أحدٍ، ومن هنا كان دقيقاً في حق قوم، وعريضاً في حق آخرین وطوله ثلاثة آلاف سنة : ألفُ صعود، وألف هبوط، وألف استواء . وفي كلام الشيخ الأكبر ما يفيد عدم التعويل على ظاهر هذه الآلاف، أن ماله الامتداد للعلق، حتى يصل إلى الجنة؛ فإنها عالية جدا.
The Bridge and the Passing Over It
Thus the Bridge – the servants' passing is varied: some safe and some differing.
His saying: 'Thus the Bridge' – 'Thus' is a forward predicate, and 'the Bridge' is a delayed subject; i.e., the Bridge is like what was mentioned of the taking of records and the weighing and the balance in terms of auditory obligation. It is with (sad) or (sin) or pure (zay) or with indication, and it was recited in the Seven (readings) with other than pure (zay). Its linguistic meaning is the clear path, derived from 'saratahu' meaning he swallowed it, because it swallows those who pass.
And legally: a bridge stretched over the surface of Hell, which the first and last come to, even the disbelievers, contrary to al-Halimi who held that they do not pass over it. Perhaps he meant the group that is thrown into Hell from the standing-place without a bridge. And it includes what was mentioned: the prophets, the truthful, and those who enter Paradise without account. All of them are silent except the prophets, who say: 'O Allah, grant safety, grant safety' – as in the Sahih.
And in some narrations: it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword, and this is the well-known view.
But al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Shaykh al-Qarafi, and others like al-Badr al-Zarkashi disputed that. They said: Assuming its authenticity, it is carried on other than its apparent meaning, being interpreted as a metaphor for severe difficulty. In that case, it does not contradict the hadiths that indicate the angels standing on its sides and the hooks in it.
Al-Qarafi added: The correct view is that it is wide, and in it are two roads: right and left. The people of happiness take the right, and the people of misery are led along the left. In it are openings, each opening leading to a layer of Hell. And some said: It becomes narrow and wide according to the narrowness and expansion of light, so the width of each person's bridge is according to the spread of his light. Indeed, the light of each person does not extend to another, so no one walks in another's light. Hence it is narrow for a group and wide for another. Its length is three thousand years: a thousand ascending, a thousand descending, and a thousand level. In the words of al-Shaykh al-Akbar (Ibn Arabi) is what indicates not relying on the apparent meaning of these thousands, for it has extension until reaching Paradise, for it is very high.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage discusses the Bridge (al-Sirat) over Hell, another essential matter of samiyyat. It begins by explaining the word 'sirat', which is pronounced with different letters. Linguistically it means the clear path, derived from the verb 'sarata' meaning to swallow, because it swallows those who pass. Legally, it is a bridge stretched over Hell, which all people come to, including disbelievers – contrary to al-Halimi's view that disbelievers do not pass over it; perhaps he meant a specific group that is cast into Hell directly from the standing place. The passage includes the prophets, truthful ones, and those entering Paradise without account. All are silent except the prophets, who supplicate for safety. The well-known description is that it is finer than a hair and sharper than a sword. However, scholars like al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, and al-Zarkashi disputed this literal interpretation, arguing that if such hadiths are authentic, they must be interpreted metaphorically to indicate extreme difficulty, so as not to contradict other hadiths about angels standing at its sides and hooks. Al-Qarafi added that the correct view is that the Sirat is wide, with two roads: right for the blessed and left for the damned, and it has openings leading to layers of Hell. Some say its narrowness and width vary according to the light of each person: each person's bridge width equals the spread of his own light, which is exclusive to him. Thus it is narrow for some and wide for others. Its length is described as 3000 years (up, down, level), but the Shaykh al-Akbar (Ibn Arabi) advises not to take these numbers literally, as the bridge extends to Paradise, which is very high.

الدقة
الكناية
النور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sirat is physically thin for all people.
Correction: Many scholars interpret the thinness as a metaphor for difficulty, and the width varies based on each person's light.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qarafi, what is the correct view of the Sirat's width?
A) It is thinner than a hair
B) It is wide with two roads
C) It is narrow for all
D) It is invisible

Key Takeaway
The Sirat's description is not to be taken literally; scholars emphasise its spiritual reality as a test.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the width of your Sirat depends on your light, what are you doing today to increase that light? Will you walk alone or with the guidance of the Prophet?
?
The Sirat is described as finer than a hair and sharper than a sword for some, yet wide for others. Which group will you be in? What specific deeds are you relying on to make your crossing easy?
?
You will cross the Sirat after your deeds are weighed. If your scale is heavy but your light is dim, how will you traverse? Are you preparing both your faith and your actions for that moment?

Definition of As-Sirat and Its Obligation
Al-Bajuri defines As-Sirat linguistically and technically, and states it is obligatory to believe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definition of As-Sirat, its obligatory status by revelation, and the differing opinion of al-Halimi.

Core Idea
As-Sirat is a real bridge over Hell that all must cross, affirmed by revelation.

In this lesson, we examine the Islamic doctrine of As-Sirat, the bridge over Hell. The author begins by clarifying its grammatical place in the poem, then gives its linguistic and technical definitions, and addresses a key disagreement. Understanding this clarifies the nature of the afterlife journey.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the definitions sink in before we break them down.
2
Understand the grammatical and pronunciation notes
Notice how the author links 'kadha' to previous matters, establishing that the Path is like the records and scales in being obligatory by revelation. Also note the permissible pronunciations of 'sirat'.
The author explains that the word can be read with sin, sad, or zay, but the seven canonical readings exclude pure zay.
3
Grasp the linguistic and technical definitions
The linguistic meaning is 'clear road' derived from 'swallowing'. The technical meaning is a bridge over Hell that all must cross. Compare these two layers.
4
Consider the disagreement with al-Halimi
Al-Halimi held that disbelievers do not cross the bridge. The author suggests a possible interpretation about a specific group. Reflect on why this matters for belief.
5
Note the behavior of the prophets on the bridge
The prophets are the only ones who speak, pleading for safety. This adds a layer of reverence and highlights the severity of the crossing.
The author cites the Sahih to support this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَذَا الصِّراط) (كذا) خبر مقدَّم، و(الصراط): مبتدأ مؤخر ؛ أي: الصراط مثل المذكور من أخذ العباد الصحف والوزن والميزان في الوجوب السمعي؛ وهو بالصاد أو بالسين أو بالزاي المحضة أو بالإشمام، وقُرئ في السبع بما عدا الزاي المحضة ومعناه لغةٌ : الطريق الواضح، مأخوذ من صرطه يصرطه إذا ابتلعه؛ لأنه يبتلع المارة . وشرعاً : جِسْرٌ ممدود على متن جهنم يَرِدُهُ الأوّلون والآخرون حتى الكفار، خلافاً للحليمي حيث ذهب إلى أنهم لا يمرون عليه، ولعله أراد الطائفة التي تُرمى في جهنم من الموقف بلا صراط وشمل ما ذكر النبيين والصديقين ومَنْ يدخل الجنة بغير حساب، وكلهم ساكتون إلا الأنبياء، فيقولون: اللهم سَلّمْ سَلّمْ ؛ كما في «الصحيح».
His saying: 'Thus the Path (as-sirat)' — (kadha) is a delayed predicate, and (as-sirat) is a delayed subject; meaning: the Path is like what was mentioned of the taking of the records, the weighing, and the scale in terms of obligatory nature by revelation (al-wujub as-sam'i). It is pronounced with sad, or sin, or pure zay, or with imam (a type of pronunciation). It is recited in the seven recitations except with pure zay. Its linguistic meaning is the clear road, derived from saratahu yasrutuhu if he swallowed it, because it swallows the passersby. Legally: it is a bridge stretched over the surface of Hell, which the first and the last will arrive at, even the disbelievers, contrary to al-Halimi who held that they do not pass over it. Perhaps he meant the group that is thrown into Hell from the standing place without a bridge, and it includes what was mentioned of the prophets, the truthful, and those who enter Paradise without account, and all of them are silent except the prophets, who say: 'O Allah, grant safety, grant safety' as in the Sahih.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'as-sirat' (the Path) and establishes its obligatory nature in Islamic belief. The author first explains the grammatical structure of the phrase 'kadha as-sirat' in the original poem, clarifying that 'kadha' refers back to previously mentioned matters like the taking of records and the weighing of deeds, all of which are obligations known by revelation (al-wujub as-sam'i). He then discusses the pronunciation of the word, noting it can be read with sad, sin, or pure zay, though the seven canonical Quranic readings do not include pure zay. The linguistic meaning of 'sirat' is a clear road, derived from the root meaning 'to swallow' because the road swallows its passers. Technically, in Islamic law, it is a bridge stretched over Hell that all creatures must cross, including disbelievers. This contradicts the opinion of al-Halimi, who thought disbelievers do not cross it; the author suggests al-Halimi may have meant a specific group thrown directly into Hell without crossing. The passage also notes that all who cross are silent except the prophets, who plead for safety.

Misconception: Some think As-Sirat is a metaphorical representation of good deeds or a narrow path only for believers.
Correction: The passage affirms it is a real bridge over Hell that all must cross, including disbelievers, and belief in it is obligatory by revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the legal (shar'an) definition of as-sirat according to this passage?

B) A bridge stretched over the surface of Hell that all people must cross
C) A narrow path leading to Paradise only for believers

Key Takeaway
The Path (as-sirat) is a real bridge over Hell that all creatures must cross, and belief in it is obligatory by revelation, not reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will cross a bridge over Hellfire. Every step you take in this life will either weigh for you or against you on that day. When you look at your daily choices—your speech, your prayers, your intentions—are you preparing for a safe crossing or a fall? What specific habit do you need to change today to make your path lighter on that bridge?
?
The prophets, who are the most beloved to Allah, will be begging for safety on that bridge. If even they cry 'O Allah, grant safety,' what about you? How often do you genuinely plead for safety from the Fire in your prayers, or do you assume it will be fine? Will you commit to adding a sincere du'a for safety from the Fire in every salah starting today?
?
Al-Bajuri mentions that all creatures, including disbelievers, will approach the bridge. The reality of that moment is certain. If you were standing at the edge of that bridge right now, what would you wish you had done differently in your life? Write down one thing you wish you had taken more seriously, and start acting on it from today.

The Thinness of the Bridge and Its Metaphorical Interpretation
Al-Bajuri presents the reported thinness and the scholarly response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over the literal thinness of the bridge and the scholars' metaphorical interpretation as a symbol of severe hardship.

Core Idea
The bridge's thinness is a metaphor for hardship, not a physical attribute.

Some narrations describe the bridge as extremely thin and sharp. However, prominent scholars like al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam and al-Qarafi argued that this cannot be taken literally, as it would contradict other authentic hadiths. They interpreted the thinness as a metaphor (kinaya) for the immense difficulty of crossing, reconciling the texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the narration about the bridge's thinness
Read the first part of the passage, which states the well-known view that the bridge is thinner than a hair and sharper than a sword.
Notice that this is described as 'the well-known view' (al-mashhur).
2
Identify the scholars who disputed it
The passage lists al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, and al-Badr az-Zarkashi as those who contested the literal interpretation.
3
Understand their metaphorical interpretation
They said that even if the narration is authentic, it should be taken figuratively as a metaphor (kinaya) for extreme difficulty, not literal thinness.
This interpretation resolves conflict with hadiths about angels standing on the bridge.
4
See how it resolves contradictions
The metaphor allows the bridge to be 'thin' in terms of difficulty while still having physical width for angels and hooks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي بعض الروايات: أنه أدقَّ من الشعرة وأحد من السيف، وهو المشهور. ونازعَ في ذلك العز بن عبد السلام والشيخ القرافي وغيرهما كالبدر الزركشي، قالوا: وعلى فرض صحة ذلك فهو محمول على غير ظاهره بأن يؤول بأنه كنايةٌ عن شدة المشقة، وحينئذٍ فلا ينافي ما ورد من الأحاديث الدالة على قيام الملائكة على جنبيه، وكون الكلاليب فيه. يسلك بهم زاد القَرَافِيُّ : والصحيح أنه عريض، وفيه طريقان يمنى ويسرى، فأهل السعادة ذات اليمين وأهل الشقاوة يسلك بهم ذات الشمال، وفيه طاقات كل طاقة تنفذ إلى طبقة من طبقات جهنم، وقال بعضهم: إنه يَدِقُ ويتسعُ بحسب ضيق النور وانتشاره فعرض صراط كل أحد بقدر انتشار نوره، فإن نور كل إنسان لا يتعداه إلى غيره، فلا يمشي أحد في نور أحدٍ، ومن هنا كان دقيقاً في حق قوم، وعريضاً في حق آخرین وطوله ثلاثة آلاف سنة : ألفُ صعود، وألف هبوط، وألف استواء . وفي كلام الشيخ الأكبر ما يفيد عدم التعويل على ظاهر هذه الآلاف، أن ماله الامتداد للعلق، حتى يصل إلى الجنة؛ فإنها عالية جدا .
In some narrations: it is thinner than a hair and sharper than a sword, and this is the well-known view. Al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, ash-Shaykh al-Qarafi and others like al-Badr az-Zarkashi disputed that. They said: Assuming its authenticity, it is interpreted figuratively, not literally, by interpreting it as a metaphor for the severity of hardship. Then it does not contradict the hadiths indicating the standing of angels on its sides and the existence of hooks on it. Al-Qarafi added: The correct view is that it is wide, and it has two paths: right and left. The people of happiness (take) the right side and the people of misery are led to the left side. It has openings, each opening leading to a layer of the layers of Hell. And some said: It narrows and widens according to the narrowness and spread of light. The width of each person's bridge is according to the spread of his light, for the light of each person does not extend to others, so no one walks in another's light. Hence it is narrow for some people and wide for others. Its length is three thousand years: a thousand ascent, a thousand descent, and a thousand level. In the words of ash-Shaykh al-Akbar (Ibn Arabi) there is what indicates not relying on the apparent meaning of these thousands: that its extension is for the creation until it reaches Paradise, for it is very high.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage discusses the physical attributes of the bridge (as-sirat) and the scholarly response to narrations about its extreme thinness. The author begins by stating that some narrations describe the bridge as thinner than a hair and sharper than a sword, which is the well-known view. However, prominent scholars like al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Qarafi, and al-Badr az-Zarkashi contested this literal interpretation. They argued that even if such narrations are authentic, they must be interpreted metaphorically (as a 'kinaya' for extreme hardship) so as not to contradict other hadiths that mention angels standing on its sides and hooks attached to it. Al-Qarafi added that the correct view is that the bridge is actually wide, with two paths—one for the blessed (right) and one for the damned (left)—and openings leading to the layers of Hell. Another opinion is that the bridge's width varies according to the person's light: each person's bridge is as wide as the spread of his light, which does not extend to others. The author then mentions that the bridge's length is said to be three thousand years (ascent, descent, level), but Ibn Arabi (ash-Shaykh al-Akbar) cautioned against taking this literally, explaining that the bridge extends until it reaches Paradise, which is very high.

Key Takeaway

?

The Width and Length of the Bridge
Al-Bajuri presents views on the bridge's width and length.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different views on the bridge's width—including its two paths and variation according to light—and the symbolic interpretation of its length.

Core Idea
The bridge's width varies by each person's light, and its length is symbolic.

After addressing the thinness narration, al-Qarafi asserts that the bridge is actually wide, with two distinct paths for the blessed and the damned. Another opinion holds that its width changes according to the spread of each person's light. The length is described as three thousand years, but caution is urged against taking this literally.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Qarafi's view on the bridge's width
Read the part stating that the correct view is that the bridge is wide, with two paths: right for the blessed and left for the damned.
Al-Qarafi also mentions openings leading to the layers of Hell.
2
Note the light-based variation
Some scholars said the bridge narrows or widens according to the narrowness or spread of each person's light. Each person's bridge width matches their light, which does not extend to others.
3
Understand the length description
The bridge's length is said to be three thousand years: a thousand ascent, a thousand descent, and a thousand level.
4
See Ibn Arabi's caution on literal length
Ibn Arabi (ash-Shaykh al-Akbar) warned against taking the thousands literally; the bridge extends until it reaches Paradise, which is very high.
This suggests the numbers may be symbolic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume the bridge has a fixed width for everyone and a literal length of three thousand years.
Correction: The bridge's width varies according to each person's light, and its length is symbolic, as cautioned by Ibn Arabi.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Arabi (ash-Shaykh al-Akbar), how should the description of the bridge's length as three thousand years be understood?
A) As a literal measurement
B) As a figurative expression not to be taken literally
C) As applying only to disbelievers
D) As a later fabrication

Key Takeaway
The bridge's width varies according to each person's light, and its length is symbolic, urging believers to focus on spiritual preparation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the width of your bridge on that Day depends entirely on the light you carry, how much of that light have you earned today? What specific act of obedience or repentance can you perform tonight to ensure your bridge is wide?
?
The bridge has a path to the left leading to misery — are there small sins you habitually commit that are quietly steering you leftward? Will you cut them off before the journey begins?
?
The length of the bridge is three thousand years of struggle: ascent, descent, and level ground. You will be alone in your own light. What one spiritual discipline have you been avoiding that would strengthen you for that journey? Decide now to begin it today, even for five minutes.

Evidence for As-Sirat and Its Interpretations
Al-Bajuri cites scriptural proofs and records alternative interpretations of the bridge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural basis for As-Sirat and the key interpretive differences between Ahl al-Sunnah and the Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
The bridge is affirmed by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, but its interpretation varies.

This lesson presents the primary evidence from the Quran and hadith for the existence of As-Sirat, the bridge over Hell. It also explores how different theological schools have understood this symbol, from literal acceptance to allegorical interpretation. By examining these views, we see how revelation and reason interact in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the section heading 'al-Sam'iyyat' indicates matters known by revelation.
2
Identify the scriptural proofs
The passage cites a Quranic verse (Ya-Seen 66) and a hadith about the bridge. Note how each is introduced.
The hadith is from the Prophet: 'The bridge will be set up across Hell...'
3
Understand the consensus and divergence
The text says the community agreed on the bridge's reality in general, but then notes two interpretive approaches: literal (Ahl al-Sunnah) and allegorical (Mu'tazilites).
The Mu'tazilites interpret it as the path to Paradise or Hell.
4
Examine the additional interpretation
A third view is mentioned: the bridge represents clear proofs, with angels questioning people and hooks on its sides.
This shows the flexibility in understanding the details of the unseen.
5
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why the author includes both the proofs and the alternative views. What does this teach about how Islamic scholars approach revealed matters?
This connects to the broader methodology of dealing with ambiguous scriptural passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات وأفاد الشعراني أنه لا يوصل لها حقيقةً، بل يوصل لمرجها الذي فيه الدرج الموصل لها ، قال : ويُوضع لهم هناك مائدة ، قال : ويقوم أحدهم فيتناول مما تدلى هناك من ثمار الجنة . وقد ورد به الكتاب قال تعالى: ﴿فَاسْتَبَقُوا الصِّرَاط) (يس: ١٦]. والسنةُ : قال : يُضرب الصراط بين ظهراني جهنم فأكون أنا وأمتي أول مَنْ يجوز » (١). ، واتفقت الكلمة عليه في الجملة؛ أي : بقطع النظر عن إبقائه على ظاهره كما هو مذهب أهل السنة وصرفه عنه، كما هو مذهب كثير من المعتزلة ؛ فإنهم ذهبوا إلى أن المراد به طريق الجنة وطريق النار . وقيل : المراد به الأدلة الواضحة وجبريل في أوله وميكائيل في وسطه، يسألان الناس عن عُمُرِهم فيما أفنوه، وعن شبابهم فيما بلوه وعن علمهم ماذا عملوا به وفي حافتيه كلاليب معلقة مأمورة تأخذ مَنْ أُمرت به.
The Sam'iyyat. And ash-Sha'rani said: It does not actually lead to it (Paradise), rather it leads to its outskirts in which are the degrees connecting to it. He said: And a table is laid for them there. He said: And one of them stands and takes from what is hung there of the fruits of Paradise. And the Book (Quran) has reported it. Allah said: 'Then they raced to the path' (Ya-Seen 66). And the Sunnah: He (the Prophet) said: 'The bridge will be set up across the middle of Hell, and I and my ummah will be the first to cross.' And the community agreed upon it in general terms, i.e., disregarding its literal interpretation as is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah, and turning away from it as is the madhhab of many of the Mu'tazila, for they held that what is meant by it is the path to Paradise and the path to Hell. And it was said: What is meant by it is the clear proofs, and Jibril is at its beginning and Mika'il at its middle, asking people about their lifespan in what they spent, and about their youth in what they wore out, and about their knowledge and what they acted upon it. And on its sides are hanging hooks commanded to take whom they are ordered to take.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage provides scriptural evidence for the reality of the bridge and mentions alternative interpretations. The section begins with 'al-Sam'iyyat' (things known by revelation). First, ash-Sha'rani is quoted saying that the bridge does not literally lead to Paradise but to its outskirts, where there are degrees and a table of fruits. Then the Quranic verse 'Then they raced to the path' (Surah Ya-Seen, 66) is cited, along with a hadith that the Prophet will be the first to cross the bridge set up over Hell. The author notes that the community (ijma') agreed on the bridge's reality in general, but there is a divergence: Ahl al-Sunnah take it literally, while many Mu'tazilites interpret it as the path to Paradise or Hell. Another opinion (qil) interprets the bridge as clear proofs, with Jibril at its beginning questioning people and Mika'il in the middle, and hooks on its sides to seize those ordered to be taken. This shows the variety of interpretations while affirming the bridge as a matter of revelation.

?
